Joint Shooting: Muslim and Magian – The Qiyas vs Istihsan Dispute
Multiple scenarios of joint shooting between Muslim and Magian

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a Magian's arrow aids a Muslim's arrow, distinguish between qiyas and istihsan on the issue, and apply the rule to similar scenarios involving timing and slaughter.

Core Idea
If a Magian's arrow aids a Muslim's arrow, the game is not lawful by istihsan, even if the Muslim's arrow alone would have killed.

When a Muslim and a Magian hunt together, the involvement of the Magian can cast doubt on the lawfulness of the game. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines several scenarios, focusing on the key intra-Hanafi dispute between qiyas (which would permit the game) and istihsan (which prohibits it). Understanding this distinction is essential for applying the principles of attribution and legal preference in hunting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary actors and actions
Read the first scenario: a Muslim shoots with tasmiyah, then a Magian's arrow hits the Muslim's arrow, causing it to deflect but still kill the game. Note who did what and the outcome.
Also note the variant with a muhrim (person in ihram) who adds force.
2
Determine the attribution of the kill
For each scenario, decide which arrow was the effective cause of death. Consider whether the first arrow alone would have reached the game.
This is the qiyas approach: attribute to the primary cause.
3
Apply istihsan to cases with Magian involvement
Even when the Muslim's arrow would have reached, the text states it is 'not lawful' by istihsan. Identify the istihsan principle: avoiding potential taint from a Magian's cooperation.
Istihsan overrides qiyas here.
4
Examine the timing scenarios
When a Muslim shoots after Magian arrows or dogs have fallen, the game may be lawful if the Muslim performs tadhkiyah (slaughter) or shoots after the Magian's tools have returned.
Contrast with shooting while the Magian's tools are still in pursuit.
5
Synthesize the common legal principle
Summarize: The game is attributed to the one whose arrow is the effective cause, but if a non-Muslim (Magian) assists in any way that contributes to the kill, the meat becomes unlawful by istihsan, except when the Muslim's action occurs after the Magian's involvement has ceased and he performs tadhkiyah.
This principle applies analogously to dogs and falcons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا رمى مسلم صيداً بسهم وسمى ثم رمى مجوسي فأصاب سهمه سهم المسلم فانحرف يمنة ويسرة إلا أنه في سننه ذلك وأصاب الصيد وقتله فالصيد للمسلم، ولكن لا ينبغي أن يأكله. ولو رمى سهم حلال سهماً إلى صيد ثم رمى محرم فأصاب سهم المحرم سهم الحلال وزاد في قوته حتى الصيد فإنه لا يحل أكله وإرسال البازي كإرسال الكلب. ولو رمى رجل صيداً بسهم وسمى الثاني الأول وأمضاه حتى أصاب ثم إن رجلاً آخر رمى ذلك الصيد بسهم فأصاب قسمی الصيد وجرحه وقتله فالمسألة على وجهين : إن كان السهم الأول بحال يعلم أنه يبلغ الصيد بدون السهم الثاني إلا أن الثاني زاد في قوته فالصيد للأول، ولم يذكر في الكتاب ما إذا كان لا يدري بأن الأول هل يبلغ الصيد لولا الثاني . قال مشايخنا وينبغي أن يكون الصيد للأول ويحل تناول هذا الصيد على كل حال ولو كان الرامي الثاني مجوسياً فأصاب سهمه سهم المسلم فإن علم أن سهم المسلم لا يصيب الصيد لولا سهم المجوسي فالصيد للمجوسي ولا يحل تناوله، ولو علم أن سهم المسلم يصيب الصيد إلا أن سهم المجوسي زاد في قوته فالصيد للمسلم ويحل تناوله قياساً ولا يحل استحساناً. ولو أن قوماً من المجوس رموا سهامهم فأقبل الصيد نحو مسلم فاراً من سهامهم فرماه المسلم وسمى فأصابه سهم المسلم وقتله فالمسألة على وجهين : إن كان المجوسي وقع على الأرض حتى رماه المسلم لم يحل أكله إلا أن يدركه المسلم ويذكيه فحينئذ يحل لأنهم أعانوه على الرمي دون حقيقة الذكاة ولم يعتبر بالرمي مع وجود حقيقة الذكاة، وإن وقعت سهام المجوسي على الأرض ثم رماه المسلم بعد ذلك وباقي المسألة بحالها حل أكله . وكذلك المجوسي إن أرسلوا كلابهم إلى صيد فأقبل الصيد هارباً فرماه السملم فقتله أو أرسل كلبه إليه فأصابه الكلب فقتله إن كان المسلم أو إرساله الكلب بعد رجوع كلاب المجوسي يحل، وإن كان حال اتباع كلابهم لا يحل وكذا لو أرسل المجوسي صقراً له أو بازياً له فهوى الصيد إلى الأرض هارباً فرماه المسلم فقتله، فإن كان رمي المسلم وإرساله حال اتباع صقر المجوسي وبازيه لا يحل، وإن كان بعد الرجوع حل. وكذا لو اتبع الصيد كلب غير معلم فأقيل الصيد فاراً منه فرماه المسلم بسهم فهو على التفصيل الذي قلنا .
When a Muslim shoots at game with an arrow and says the tasmiyah, then a Magian shoots and his arrow hits the Muslim's arrow and turns it right and left, but it is on its course and hits the game and kills it, the game belongs to the Muslim, but he should not eat it. And if a licit person shoots an arrow at game, then an illicit person (muhrim) shoots and his arrow hits the licit arrow and increases its force until the game, then it is not lawful to eat it. Sending a falcon is like sending a dog. And if a man shoots at game with an arrow and says the tasmiyah, then another man shoots that game with an arrow and hits the game and wounds it and kills it, the case has two aspects: If the first arrow was in a state that it is known that it would reach the game without the second arrow, but the second increased its force, then the game belongs to the first. He did not mention in the book the case when it is not known whether the first would have reached the game without the second. Our scholars said: It should be that the game belongs to the first, and it is permissible to eat this game in any case. And if the second shooter was a Magian and his arrow hit the Muslim's arrow, then if it is known that the Muslim's arrow would not have reached the game without the Magian's arrow, then the game belongs to the Magian and it is not lawful to eat it. And if it is known that the Muslim's arrow would have reached the game, but the Magian's arrow increased its force, then the game belongs to the Muslim and it is lawful to eat it by qiyas, but not lawful by istihsan. And if a group of Magians shoot their arrows, and the game comes towards a Muslim fleeing from their arrows, and the Muslim shoots and says the tasmiyah, and his arrow hits the game and kills it, the case has two aspects: If the Magian's arrows had fallen to the ground before the Muslim shot, then it is not lawful to eat it unless the Muslim catches up and slaughters it (tadhkiyah); then it is lawful because they assisted in the shooting not in the actual slaughter, and the shooting is not considered in the presence of actual slaughter. And if the Magian's arrows fell on the ground and then the Muslim shot afterwards, the rest of the case being the same, his eating it is lawful. Similarly, if Magians send their dogs to game, and the game comes fleeing, and the Muslim shoots and kills it, or sends his dog and it catches and kills it, if the Muslim's shot or sending was after the return of the Magian's dogs, it is lawful; if it was while they were pursuing, it is not lawful. Likewise, if a Magian sends his falcon or baz, and the game descends to the ground fleeing, and the Muslim shoots and kills it, then if the Muslim's shot and sending was while the Magian's falcon or baz was pursuing, it is not lawful; if after its return, it is lawful. Similarly, if a dog that is not trained (ghayr mu'allam) pursues the game, and the game comes fleeing from it, and the Muslim shoots it with an arrow, then the ruling is according to the detail we have said.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This long passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with complex cases where a Muslim and a Magian (or a muhrim) shoot together. The general principle is that the game is attributed to the shooter whose arrow was the effective cause, provided the other did not merely assist. Several scenarios are examined: (1) A Muslim shoots, then a Magian's arrow hits the Muslim's arrow, causing it to deflect but still hit the game. The game is attributed to the Muslim, but he should not eat it, likely due to the involvement of the Magian. (2) A muhrim's arrow increases the force of a halal arrow; the game is not lawful. (3) Two Muslims shoot, and the second arrow adds force; the game belongs to the first if the first would have reached anyway. If uncertain, the preferred view is that the game belongs to the first and is lawful. (4) When a Magian aids a Muslim's arrow: if the Muslim's arrow would not have reached without the Magian's, the game belongs to the Magian and is unlawful; if the Muslim's arrow would have reached but the Magian's added force, qiyas says it is lawful for the Muslim but istihsan says it is not. (5) If Magians shoot and their arrows have fallen, and then a Muslim shoots, it is lawful only if the Muslim catches and slaughters the animal; if he shoots while the Magian arrows are in the air or pursuing, it is not lawful. The same applies to dogs and falcons: if the Muslim acts after the Magian's animals have returned, it is lawful; if during their pursuit, it is not. The passage ends with a similar rule for an untrained dog.

Key Terms
محرم (muhrim): A person in a state of ihram (pilgrim) who is forbidden from hunting.
مجوسي (majusi): A Magian (Zoroastrian), considered a non-Muslim with a separate legal status.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of legal reasoning that abandons qiyas in favor of a more suitable ruling based on public interest or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A Muslim shoots an arrow at a deer. A Magian's arrow hits the Muslim's arrow and increases its force, but the Muslim's arrow would have reached the deer anyway. According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, is the deer lawful to eat?
A) Yes, because the Muslim's arrow was the effective cause
B) No, because of istihsan considering the Magian's aid
C) Yes, only if the Magian says tasmiyah
D) No, because the Magian's arrow hit first

Key Takeaway
When a Magian's arrow aids a Muslim's arrow in killing game, even if the Muslim's arrow would have killed alone, the meat is not lawful to eat by the Hanafi principle of istihsan.

When Game Falls on Ground or Objects: Halal or Haram?
Determines halal/haram based on landing surface avoidability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for game that falls on different surfaces after being shot and apply the principle of avoidability to determine halal or haram.

Core Idea
Game is halal only if the landing surface is unavoidable; avoidable lethal surfaces make it haram.

Eating game killed by a hunting arrow is generally lawful, but what if the animal falls onto something before dying? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on whether the hunter could have avoided the landing surface. In this passage, the commentator expands on the initial ruling, clarifying that surfaces like ground, rope, and roof are unavoidable, while trees, walls, and erected weapons are not. Understanding this distinction prevents mistakenly deeming halal what should be haram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Start with the case of game falling directly on the ground. The author states it is halal. Note the rationale: the hunter cannot prevent the animal from hitting the ground, so any harm from the ground is disregarded lest the door to lawful hunting be closed.
Ground is considered unavoidable.
2
Extend to similar unavoidable surfaces
The author extends the same ruling to rope, roof, and a brick placed on the ground (not raised). These are also unavoidable — the animal cannot avoid them once shot. Therefore, they are treated like the ground: halal.
The key is that the surface is low and not a potential weapon.
3
Identify the exception: avoidable surfaces
When the game falls on a tree, wall, standing spear, upright reed, or edge of a brick — or is on a mountain and falls — it becomes haram. These surfaces are avoidable because the hunter could choose not to shoot when the animal is near them. Moreover, they may cause death by their sharpness or the fall itself.
The possibility that the surface killed the animal creates uncertainty that tilts to prohibition.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The reasoning is based on balancing probabilities and the Shari'ah preference for prohibition when both halal and haram are possible and avoidance is feasible. If the surface is unavoidable, the doubt is overridden; if avoidable, the doubt leads to prohibition.
This principle is applied in many fiqh rulings: 'When halal and haram conflict, haram is given precedence if avoidance is possible.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن وقع على الأرض ابتداء (حل لأنه لا يمكنه التحرز عنه فسقط اعتباره لئلا ينسد بابه على ما بيناه بخلاف ما إذا أمكن التحرز عنه لأن اعتباره لا يؤدي إلى سد بابه وإلى اعتباره لا يؤدي إلى الجرح فأمكن ترجيح المحرم عند التعارض على ما هو الأصل في الشرع ولو وقع على حبل أو سطح أو آجرة موضوعة فاستقر ولم يترد حل لأن وقوعه على هذه الأشياء كوقوعه على الأرض ابتداء، ولأنه لا يمكن الاحتراز عنه فسقط اعتباره بخلاف ما إذا وقع على شجر أو حائط أو أجرة ثم وقع على الأرض أو رماه وهو على جبل فتردى منه إلى الأرض أو رماه فوقع على رمح منصوب أو قصبة قائمة أو على حرف آجرة حيث يحرم لاحتمال أن أحد هذه الأشياء قتله بحده أو بترديته وهو ممكن الاحتراز عنه . وقال في المنتقى : لو رمى صيداً فوقع على صخرة فانفلق رأسه أو انشق بطنه لم يؤكل
He (the author), may Allah have mercy on him, said: '(And if it falls on the ground directly, it is lawful) because it cannot avoid it, so its consideration falls away lest the door be closed, as we explained, contrary to when it is possible to avoid it, because its consideration does not lead to closing the door, and its consideration does not lead to wounding, so it is possible to prefer the prohibited when there is conflict, as is the principle in the Shari'ah. And if it falls on a rope or a roof or a placed brick and settles and does not fall further, it is lawful, because its falling on these things is like falling on the ground directly, and because it cannot avoid it, so its consideration falls away, contrary to when it falls on a tree or a wall or a brick and then falls to the ground, or he shoots it while it is on a mountain and it falls from it to the ground, or he shoots it and it falls on an erected spear or a standing reed or on the edge of a brick, where it is prohibited due to the possibility that one of these things killed it by its edge or by the fall, and it is possible to avoid it. And it is said in al-Muntaqa: If he shoots game and it falls on a rock and its head splits or its belly splits, it is not eaten.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This final passage clarifies the distinction: if a hunted animal falls directly on the ground after being shot, it is halal because it cannot avoid hitting the ground. Similarly, if it falls on a rope, roof, or a brick placed on the ground and stays there without falling further, it is also halal because these objects are like the ground and unavoidable. However, if it falls on a tree, wall, or brick and then falls to the ground, or if it is on a mountain and falls down, or if it falls on a standing spear or reed, it is haram because these objects could have caused death by their sharp edge or by the fall, and the hunter can avoid shooting in such situations. The ruling is based on balancing probabilities: when the cause of death is uncertain but a possible lethal factor is avoidable, the game is prohibited. Al-Muntaqa adds that if the animal falls on a rock and its head splits or belly splits, it is not eaten, as that indicates death by the rock rather than the arrow.

Key Terms
متردية (mutaraddiyah): An animal that dies from falling from a height; in this context, falling onto a sharp or elevated object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter shoots a bird that falls onto a roof and then dies. The roof is low and not sharp. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Halal, because the roof is unavoidable
B) Halal, because the arrow wound is fatal
C) Haram, because it fell on a man-made structure
D) Haram, because the roof is not the ground

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling depends on whether the landing surface is unavoidable and not a potential lethal object; avoidable surfaces that could kill render the game haram.

Ruling on cutting a limb from a living game animal
States the ruling on cut limbs and three cuts, then explains reasoning reconciling textual sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a limb cut from a living game animal and explain the underlying principle that distinguishes between certain and probable cause of death.

Core Idea
A limb cut from a living game animal is carrion; the rest is halal unless three or more cuts from the rump indicate certain death.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on hunting animals when a limb is cut from the living prey. The primary ruling distinguishes between the cut limb (forbidden) and the rest of the animal (halal), but an exception occurs when multiple cuts from the hind part indicate certain death from the hunt. The commentary resolves an apparent textual contradiction by clarifying the principle of certainty over probability in determining the cause of death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The matn gives two cases: (a) cutting a single limb—the limb is haram, the rest halal; (b) cutting three or more from the rump—the whole is halal.
The key is the location and number of cuts.
2
Understand the underlying reason
The limb is forbidden due to the possibility that the animal died from another cause (e.g., shock or weight) rather than the hunting wound. This creates doubt.
This applies the principle that certainty is not overridden by doubt.
3
Analyze the reconciliation
Al-Hakim Abu al-Fadl and al-Sarakhsi reconcile the apparent contradiction between al-Asl (absolute prohibition) and al-Muntaqa (the above ruling) by distinguishing cases: when death is apparent (head split, belly burst) the animal is halal; when death is conjectural, it is forbidden.
The absolute answer in al-Asl applied to cases where the hunting tool directly caused death.
4
Apply the legal maxim
The principle 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' (al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk) governs: if the cause of death is certain (apparent), the animal is halal; if only probable, it is haram due to the possibility of another cause.
This is a foundational maxim in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصيد
عضواً منه أكل الصيد لا العضو وإن قطعه ثلاثاً والأكثر مما يلي العجز أكل كله وحرم هو لاحتمال موته بسبب آخر. قال الحاكم أبو الفضل رحمه الله تعالى وهذا خلاف إطلاق الجواب المذكور في الأصل فيما عدا هذا المفسر لأن حصول الموت بانفلاق الرأس وانشقاق البطن ظاهر وبالرمي موهوم فيتردد فالظاهر أولى بالاعتبار من الموهوم فيحرم بخلاف ما إذا لم ينشق ولم ينفلق لأن موته بالرمي الظاهر فلا يحرم، ولا يحمل إطلاق الجواب في الأصل عليه، وحمل السرخسي ما ذكر في المنتقى على ما إذا أصابه حد الصخرة فانشق كذلك، وحمل المذكور في الأصل على أنه إذا لم يصبه من الصخرة إلا ما يصيبه من الأرض أو وقع عليه فحمل كذلك فكلا التأويلين صحيح ومعناهما واحد لأن كلاً منهما يحمل ما ذكره في الأصل على ما إذا مات بالرمي، وما ذكره في المنتقى على ما إذا مات بغيره، وفي لفظ المنتقى إشارة إليه ألا ترى أنه قال «الاحتمال الموت بسبب آخر» أي غير الرمي وهذا يرجع إلى اختلاف اللفظ دون المعنى ولا يبالي به.
Book of Hunting. A limb from it: the game is eaten, not the limb. And if he cuts it three times or more from the side of the rump, the whole is eaten. And it [the limb] is forbidden due to the possibility of its death by another cause. Al-Hakim Abu al-Fadl, may Allah have mercy on him, said: This contradicts the absolute answer mentioned in al-Asl except for this specific interpretation, because the occurrence of death by splitting of the head and bursting of the belly is apparent, while by shooting it is conjectural, so one is uncertain; the apparent is more worthy of consideration than the conjectural, so it is forbidden, unlike when it does not split or burst because its death by shooting is apparent, so it is not forbidden. And the absolute answer in al-Asl is not applied to it. Al-Sarakhsi interpreted what is mentioned in al-Muntaqa as when the edge of the rock hits it and it splits thus. And he interpreted what is mentioned in al-Asl as when it is not struck by the rock except as it would be struck by the ground, or if it falls on it, so it is interpreted thus. So both interpretations are correct and their meaning is one, because each of them interprets what he mentioned in al-Asl as when it dies by shooting, and what he mentioned in al-Muntaqa as when it dies by something else. And in the wording of al-Muntaqa there is an indication of that; do you not see that he said 'the possibility of death by another cause' meaning other than shooting? This comes down to a difference in wording without difference in meaning, and no attention is paid to it.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the chapter on hunting from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It presents two rulings from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq: (1) if a hunter cuts off a limb from a living game animal, the rest of the animal is halal but the cut limb is not (it is considered carrion); (2) if he cuts off three or more pieces from the hind part (the rump area), the entire animal becomes halal. The reason for forbidding the single limb is the possibility that the animal may have died from another cause (like the shock of the cut) rather than from the act of hunting itself. The commentary then addresses a potential contradiction with the statement in al-Asl (the original source) which apparently gave an absolute answer that the animal is forbidden in such cases. Al-Hakim Abu al-Fadl explains that the contradiction is only apparent: when the animal's head is split open or its belly is ripped (a clear fatal injury), death is certain from the hunting tool, so it is halal; but when death is merely probable (like from the limb cut), the animal is forbidden due to doubt. Al-Sarakhsi reconciles the two sources by interpreting that in al-Muntaqa the animal's death is caused by a rock-edge cut, while in al-Asl the animal is not cut by a sharp rock but merely struck with force, so the rulings differ. The core principle is that certainty (of death by hunting) outweighs mere probability.

Key Terms
عضواً (udwan): A limb or part cut from a living game animal.
لاحتمال موته بسبب آخر (lihtimal mawtihi bi sababin akhar): Due to the possibility that the animal died from another cause (e.g., shock, weight, or impact) rather than the cutting wound.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a hunter cuts off a single limb from a living game animal, what is the ruling on the rest of the animal?
A) The entire animal is halal
B) The entire animal is haram
C) The cut limb is haram but the rest is halal
D) The rest is halal only if three or more cuts are made

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi hunting law, a single cut limb from a living game animal is carrion (haram) due to possible other cause of death, but the rest is halal; only when three or more cuts from the rump indicate certain death does the whole become halal.

Ruling on a Water Bird When the Wound is Submerged
Specific ruling on water birds with immersed wounds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific ruling for a hunted water bird whose wound is immersed in water and explain the underlying reasoning (talil) of the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
If the wound of a hunted water bird is submerged, doubt about the cause of death makes it impermissible to eat.

Hunting rulings require certainty that the hunting tool caused death. When a water bird is shot over water, immersion of the wound introduces doubt: did the arrow kill, or did the bird drown from water entering the wound? This lesson unpacks a concise Hanafi ruling that addresses this scenario, highlighting how the school protects the principle of certainty in halal consumption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that this ruling applies specifically to a bird that lives in or near water (al-tayr al-marma ma'iyan) and is shot with an arrow. The key factor is whether the wound becomes submerged in water.
The bird is not necessarily aquatic; it could be any bird shot over water.
2
State the ruling for each case
If the wound is not immersed, the bird is halal and may be eaten. If the wound is immersed, the bird is not eaten (haram). Note that the ruling changes based on the condition of immersion.
The ruling is binary: halal when dry, haram when wet.
3
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The text gives the reason: because the bird 'drinks water into the wound' causing increased pain, so death could be attributed to the water rather than the shot. This creates doubt (shakk) about the cause of death, which makes the animal impermissible.
The principle is that certainty (yaqin) in the cause of death is required for halal consumption.
4
Analyze the analogy (qiyas)
The text says the immersed wound 'becomes like when an arrow hits it' – meaning the water acts as an independent harming agent, similar to a second strike. This analogy reinforces that the arrow alone cannot be credited with the kill.
The analogy strengthens the ruling by equating immersion to a separate cause of harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان الطير المرمى مائياً فإن لم تنغمس الجراحة في الماء أكل، وإن انغمست لا تؤكل لاحتمال الموت به دون الرمي لأنه يشرب الجرح الماء فيسبب زيادة الألم فصار كما إذا أصابه السهم
And if the shot bird is a water bird, then if the wound is not immersed in water, it is eaten; and if it is immersed, it is not eaten due to the possibility of death by it [the water] rather than by the shot, because it [the bird] drinks water into the wound, causing increased pain, so it becomes like when an arrow hits it.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage provides a specific ruling for water birds killed by an arrow. The principle is that the cause of death must be the hunting tool, not some external factor. If the wound is not submerged, death is attributed to the shot, so the bird is halal. If the wound is submerged, water enters the wound, which could cause death by drowning or increased pain rather than the shot. This creates doubt, so the bird is not eaten. The reasoning is that the bird 'drinks' water into the wound, causing additional harm, analogous to being hit by an arrow in the water.

Key Terms
الطير المرمى مائياً (al-tayr al-marma ma'iyan): A bird that lives in water or is shot over water, such that its wound may become submerged.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a water bird not eaten if its wound is immersed in water?
A) Because water birds are inherently haram
B) Because it might have drowned instead of dying from the shot
C) Because the arrow becomes impure in water
D) Because immersion makes the wound heal

Key Takeaway
Certainty that the hunting tool caused death is required for halal consumption; immersion of the wound in water creates doubt and thus renders the animal impermissible.

Hunting with Mi'rad and Pellet: The Cutting Wound Requirement
Ruling with hadith evidence on hunting kills

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game killed by the side of a mi'rad or a pellet, and identify the hadith evidence that distinguishes between a valid cutting wound and blunt-force killing.

Core Idea
Only a cutting wound validates a hunt; blunt-force kills are haram.

In this lesson, we examine a classical Hanafi ruling with direct prophetic proof. The text addresses a crucial distinction: not every kill by a hunting tool is permissible—the manner of death matters. Using two authentic hadiths, the author establishes that a valid hunt requires a wound (jarh) that allows blood to flow, invalidating kills that crush or strike without cutting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The passage rules on game killed by the side (width) of a mi'rad (a broad-head arrow) or by a pellet (bunduqah). Such game is haram (forbidden). Distinguish this from a kill by the sharp edge of the mi'rad, which is halal.
2
Examine the First Hadith
Consider the hadith of 'Adi ibn Hatim: the Prophet explicitly allowed eating game when the mi'rad pierces (fakhazaqa, causing a cut) and forbade it when it strikes with its side. The imperative 'fakul' indicates permission; the prohibition 'la ta'kul' indicates prohibition.
The key linguistic distinction is between 'khazaqa' (piercing cut) and 'ard' (side strike).
3
Examine the Second Hadith and Rationale
The Prophet forbade al-hadhf (throwing pebbles) stating they do not properly hunt but break bones and gouge eyes. This emphasizes that a valid kill requires a wound that causes bloodshed. The pellet and the side of the mi'rad kill by blunt force, not by cutting.
The underlying rationale (illa) is the absence of a cutting wound (jarh).
4
Apply the Ruling
Given that a pellet or side strike does not produce a cutting wound, the meat is haram. Conversely, if the sharp edge of the mi'rad cuts, the kill is halal. This ruling applies to modern tools: any projectile that kills by crushing or blunt force (e.g., a blunt crossbow bolt) yields haram meat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (وما قتله المعراض بعرضه أو البندقة حرم لما رويناه من حديث ابراهيم، ولما روي أن عدي بن حاتم قال للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : إني أرمي الصيد بالمعراض فأصيب فقال : إذا رميت بالمعراض فخزقت فكله وإن أصابه بعرضه فلا تأكله. رواه البخاري ومسلم وأحمد ولما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام نهى عن الحذف وقال : إنها لا تصيد ولكنها تكسر العظم وتفقأ العين. رواه البخاري ومسلم وأحمد ولأن الجرح لا بد منه لما بينا من قبل والبندقة لا تجرح، وكذا عرض المعراض والمعراض سهم لا ريش ولا نصل له وإنما هو حديد الرأس الحديد سمي معراضاً لأنه يذهب معترضاً وتارة يصيب بعرضه وتارة يصيب بحده.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And what is killed by the mi'rad with its side, or by the pellet, is forbidden because of what we narrated from the hadith of Ibrahim, and because of what was narrated that 'Adi ibn Hatim said to the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace: I shoot game with the mi'rad and hit. He said: When you shoot with the mi'rad and it pierces, eat it; and if it strikes with its side, do not eat it. Narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad. And because of what was narrated that he, peace be upon him, forbade throwing pebbles and said: They do not hunt, but they break the bone and gouge the eye. Narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad. And because a wound is necessary, as we have explained before, and the pellet does not wound, and likewise the side of the mi'rad. The mi'rad is an arrow that has no feathers and no arrowhead; it is only an iron head; it is called mi'rad because it goes sideways; sometimes it hits with its side and sometimes with its edge.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It explicitly forbids eating game killed by the side (width) of a mi'rad (a type of arrow without feathers or arrowhead, with an iron head that often hits sideways) or by a pellet (bunduqah, a small ball shot from a sling or gun). The evidence is from two authentic hadiths: (1) 'Adi ibn Hatim's hadith where the Prophet instructed that if the mi'rad pierces (cuts) the game, it is halal, but if it strikes with the broad side, it is not. (2) The Prophet forbade 'hadhf' (throwing stones/pebbles) saying they do not properly hunt but only break bones and gouge eyes. The underlying rationale is that a valid kill in hunting requires a wound (jarh) that allows blood to flow, and the side of the mi'rad or a pellet does not produce a cutting wound; they kill by blunt force, which is not a valid form of hunting. The mi'rad is defined: it is a special arrow without fletching or a typical arrowhead, but with an iron head that may strike edge-first (producing a wound) or side-first (not producing a wound). Only if it hits with its sharp edge does it count as a valid hunting tool.

Key Terms
المعراض (al-mi'rad): A specific type of arrow without feathers or an arrowhead, but with an iron head; it often flies sideways and may strike with its edge or its side.
البندقة (al-bunduqah): A pellet or small ball used in slings or guns that kills by blunt force, not by cutting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited, what should one do if a hunt is killed by the side (width) of a mi'rad?
A) Eat it, because it is killed by a hunting tool
B) Not eat it, because it did not cause a cutting wound
C) Eat it only if no other food is available
D) It is makruh but not haram

Key Takeaway
A hunting kill is only halal if the tool causes a cutting wound; kills by blunt force (side of mi'rad or pellet) are haram.

Hunting Instruments: The Principle of Cutting vs. Weight
Rulings on different hunting tools based on cutting vs. weight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of jarh (cutting) versus thiqal (weight) to determine the permissibility of game killed by various hunting instruments.

Core Idea
A hunted animal is halal only if it dies from a cutting wound, not from blunt force or weight.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a series of rulings on different hunting tools. The underlying principle is that the animal must be killed by a wound that cuts (jarh), not by weight or pressure (thiqal). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly assessing the halal status of game in various scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Principle
Understand that the determining factor is whether death resulted from a cutting wound (jarh) or from weight/blunt force (thiqal).
If doubt exists, the meat is not halal.
2
Analyze Sword/Knife
If the sharp edge hits, it cuts → halal. If the blunt side hits, it kills by weight → not halal.
3
Analyze Stones
A heavy stone kills by weight → haram. A light stone with a sharp edge that cuts → halal (certainty of death by cutting).
A long sharp stone like an arrow is treated like a sword.
4
Analyze Iron Piece (Marwah) and Stick
An iron piece that crushes (does not cut deeply) kills by crushing → haram. Even if it cuts veins, doubt remains due to possible prior death by weight. A stick without a sharp edge kills by weight → haram; but if it has a sharp edge and cuts, it is like a sword.
5
Generalize the Principle
Whenever death is certain to be by cutting, it is halal; if by weight or doubtful, it is not halal (either definitively or out of caution).
This is the overarching rule (asl) for such cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن رماه بالسكين أو السيف فإن أصابه بحده أكل وإلا فلا وإن رماه بحجر فإن كان ثقيلاً لا يؤكل . وإن لاحتمال أنه قتله بثقله، وإن كان الحجر خفيفاً وله حد وجرح لتيقن الموت بالحجر حينئذ. ولو جعل الحجر طويلاً كالسهم وهو خفيف وبه حده ورمى به صيداً فإن جرح حل لقتله بجرحه، ولو رماه بمروة حديدة فلم يبضع بضعاً لا يحل لأنه قتله دقاً، وكذا إذا رماه بها فقطع أوداجه وأبان رأسه لأن العروق قد تنقطع بالثقل فيقع الشك، ويحتمل مات قبل قطع الأوداج. ولو رماه بعود مثل العصا ونحوه لا يحل لأنه قتله ثقلاً لا جرحاً إلا إذا كان له حد بضع بضعاً فيكون كالسيف والرمح والأصل في جنس هذه المسائل أن الموت إذا حصل بالجرح يتعين حل، وإن حصل بالثقل أو شكل فيه فلا يحل حتماً أو احتياطاً.
And if he shoots it with a knife or a sword, then if it hits with its edge, it is eaten; otherwise, it is not. And if he shoots it with a stone, if it is heavy, it is not eaten, due to the possibility that he killed it by its weight. And if the stone is light and has an edge and it wounds, then the certainty of death by the stone is established at that time. And if he makes the stone long like an arrow, it is light and has an edge, and he shoots it at game, and it wounds, it is halal because he killed it by its wound. And if he shoots it with a piece of iron that does not cut deeply, it is not halal because he killed it by crushing. Similarly, if he shoots it with it and it cuts his jugular veins and severs his head, because the veins may be cut by the weight, so doubt occurs, and it is possible that it died before the cutting of the veins. And if he shoots it with a stick like a rod and the like, it is not halal because he killed it by weight, not by wound; except if it has an edge that cuts deeply, then it is like a sword and a spear. The general principle in this type of issue is that if death occurs by the wound, it is definitely halal; and if it occurs by weight or is doubtful, then it is not halal, either definitively or out of caution.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings on various hunting tools, all following a single principle: death must be caused by a cutting wound, not by blunt force or weight. For a knife or sword, if it hits with the sharp edge, the game is halal; if it hits with the flat side, it is not. For stones: a heavy stone kills by weight, so it is not halal; but a light stone with a sharp edge that cuts is valid because death is certain to be by cutting. If a long light stone is made arrow-like, it is valid if it wounds. A piece of iron ('marwah') that does not cut deeply is not valid because it kills by crushing; even if it happens to cut the jugular veins, there is doubt because the weight might have caused the cut after death. A stick or rod is invalid unless it has a sharp edge, in which case it is like a sword. The overarching principle (asl fi jins hadhihi al-masa'il) is that if death is from the wound, it is halal; if from weight or doubtful, it is not.

Key Terms
ثقلا (thiqalan): Killing by weight or pressure, not by cutting.
جرح (jarh): A wound that cuts the skin and lets blood flow, the essential requirement for a valid kill in hunting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter throws a heavy stone at a bird and kills it. According to the Hanafi school, is it halal?
A) Yes, because the stone killed it
B) No, because the stone kills by weight, not by cutting
C) Yes, but only if the stone has an edge
D) It is makruh tanzihi

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires that the death of game be caused by a cutting wound (jarh); if it is from weight (thiqal) or doubtful, the meat is not halal.

Dispute on the necessity of blood flow from the wound
Intra-Hanafi dispute on blood flow requirement in hunting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two intra-Hanafi positions on whether blood flow is required for hunted game to be halal, and identify the proof cited by each side.

Core Idea
Blood flow from the wound is required by some Hanafis; others consider the wound itself sufficient.

This passage presents a dispute among early Hanafi scholars regarding the requirement of blood flow when hunting. The shared premise is that if blood flows, the meat is halal by consensus. The disagreement arises when no blood is visible. Understanding this dispute refines your ability to distinguish between essential conditions (shurut) and extra conditions debated within the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
State the common ground
The passage begins by noting consensus (bil-ittifaq) when the wound is bleeding (mudmiyan). This establishes the baseline that blood flow makes the meat halal without dispute.
2
Identify the point of divergence
The disagreement is when the wound is not bleeding. Two positions emerge: Isma'il al-Saffar requires blood flow; Abu Bakr al-Iskaf considers the wound alone sufficient.
Both scholars are from within the Hanafi school.
3
Examine the proof for the requirement
The hadith 'anhar al-dama bima shi'ta' (spill the blood by whatever you wish) is cited. The imperative indicates obligation. Also, the essence of dhabh is extraction of blood.
Hadith narrated by Ahmad and Abu Dawud.
4
Examine the proof against requirement
The hunter did what was within his capacity (the wound); blood flow is not under his control because blood may be withheld or the wound is narrow.
This argument relies on the principle of not burdening beyond capacity.
5
Note the supporting evidence
The ruling on an arrow hitting the hoof or horn: if blood flows, it is halal; otherwise not. This supports the requirement view.
The author uses this to strengthen the position of Isma'il al-Saffar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن جرحه فمات كان الجرح مدمياً حل بالاتفاق، وإن كان غير مدم اختلفوا فيه؛ قيل لا يحل لانعدام معنى الذكاة وهو إخراج الدم النجس وشرط النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إخراج الدم بقوله انهر الدم بما شئت (۱) رواه أحمد وأبو داود وغيرهما. وقيل يحل لإتيانه ما في وسعه وهو الجرح وإخراج الدم ليس من وسعه فلا يكون مكلفاً به لأن الدم قد ينحبس بقتله أو لضيق المنفذ بين العروق وقد قدمنا . وإن ذبح الشاة ولم يخرج منها الدم قيل يحل أكلها، وقيل لا يحل، فالأول قول أبي بكر الإسكاف والثاني قول اسمعيل الصفار، ووجه القولين دخل فيما ذكرنا وإن أصاب السهم ظلف الصيد أو قرنه فإن أدماه حل وإلا فلا، وهذا يؤيد قول من يشترط خروج الدم.
And if he wounds it and it dies, and the wound is bleeding, it is halal by consensus. And if it is not bleeding, they differed about it. It is said it is not halal due to the absence of the meaning of dhabh, which is the extraction of impure blood, and the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, stipulated the extraction of blood by his saying: 'Spill the blood by whatever you wish' narrated by Ahmad and Abu Dawud and others. And it is said it is halal because he did what was within his capacity, which is the wound, and extracting the blood is not within his capacity, so he is not held responsible for it, because blood may be withheld by its killing or due to the narrowness of the passage between the veins, and we have already mentioned. And if he slaughters a sheep and no blood comes out of it, it is said its meat is halal to eat, and it is said it is not halal. The first is the saying of Abu Bakr al-Iskaf, and the second is the saying of Isma'il al-Saffar. And the justification for the two sayings enters into what we have mentioned. And if an arrow hits the hoof or horn of the game, and it causes blood, it is halal; otherwise no. And this supports the saying of those who stipulate the exit of blood.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: when a hunter wounds an animal and it dies, but no blood flows from the wound, is the meat halal? There is unanimous agreement that if blood flows, it is halal. The disagreement arises when no blood is seen. One group (the view of Isma'il al-Saffar) says it is not halal, because the essence of dhabh (Islamic slaughter) is the extraction of blood, and the Prophet commanded spilling blood. The other group (the view of Abu Bakr al-Iskaf) says it is halal because the hunter fulfilled his obligation by making a wound; blood flow is not under his control. The commentator also mentions the analogous case of slaughtering a sheep that does not bleed. Then another ruling is given: if an arrow hits the hoof or horn and causes blood, it is halal; otherwise not. This latter ruling supports the view that blood flow is required. The passage does not explicitly state which view is mufta bihi, but the mention of the latter ruling 'supports' suggests the requirement of blood is stronger.

Key Terms
مدمياً (mudmiyan): The wound is bleeding or has blood flowing from it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consensus ruling in the Hanafi school if a hunter wounds an animal and blood flows from the wound?
A) It is halal by consensus
B) It is haram by consensus
C) There is disagreement
D) It is makruh

Key Takeaway
The requirement of blood flow in hunting is an intra-Hanafi dispute; the author leans towards requiring blood flow.

Hanafi Ruling on Cut Limb from Game and the Three-Cut Exception
Presents the Shafi'i objection and Hanafi response regarding cut limbs, then the ruling on three cuts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a limb cut from a living game animal, explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection, and distinguish the three-cut ruling from the single cut.

Core Idea
A limb cut from a living animal is carrion — legal life persists until dhabh at death.

This passage presents a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i on the status of a limb cut from a game animal that then dies. The Hanafi school, relying on a prophetic hadith, holds the limb is carrion (haram). Imam al-Shafi'i argues it is halal if the animal dies from the cut, considering it part of an emergency slaughter (dhabh al-idtirar). The passage also clarifies that when three or more cuts are made from the rump, the entire animal becomes halal, as the separation is only in form, not in legal ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and the Opposing View
State the Hanafi ruling: the limb is not eaten, the rest of the animal is eaten. Then state the Shafi'i position: the limb is eaten if the animal dies from it.
This is the core dispute.
2
Analyze the Hanafi Proof
Locate the hadith cited: 'Whatever is cut from a living animal is carrion.' Understand that 'living' here means alive both literally and legally at the time of cutting.
The hadith is from Ibn Majah, a hadith ahad, but its meaning is clear (qat' al-dalalah).
3
Understand the Shafi'i Proof and the Hanafi Response
The Shafi'i argument: the limb is separated by dhabh al-idtirar (slaughter under duress), so it is like meat from a proper slaughter. The Hanafi response: at the moment of cutting, the animal is still alive; dhabh only occurs at death, by which time the limb is already dead and separated.
The Hanafi response emphasizes the temporal sequence.
4
Examine the Usuli Note on Absolute and Restricted
The commentator mentions a principle that 'the absolute applies to the perfect' but notes it conflicts with usul. Reflect on how the absolute term 'living' in the hadith is understood without restriction.
This is an advanced usuli nuance; focus on the ruling for now.
5
Understand the Three-Cut Exception
If three or more cuts are made from the rump side, the animal is considered dead immediately from the wounds. Hence, the whole animal is halal because the separation is only in form, not in legal ruling.
This exception shows how the ruling changes when life is no longer conceivable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (وإن رمى صيداً فقطع عضواً . أكل الصيد لا العضو) وقال الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه : أكل إن مات الصيد منه لأنه مبان بذكاة الاضطرار فيحل كالمبان بذكاة الاختيار بخلاف ما إذا لم يمت لأنه ما أبين بالذكاة. ولنا قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام ما قطع من بهيمة وهي حية فما قطع منها فهو ميتة» رواه ابن ماجه ذكر الحي مطلقاً فينصرف إلى الحي حقيقة وحكماً العضو المبان بهذه الصفة لأن المبان منه حي حقيقة لقيام الحياة فيه، وكذا حكماً لأنه هذه سلامته بعد الجراحة ولهذا اعتبر هذا القدر من الحياة حتى لو وقع في الماء وفيه هذا القدر من الحياة يحرم بخلاف ما إذا أبين بذكاة الاختيار لأن المبان منه ميت حكماً ألا ترى أنه لو وقع في هذه الحالة في الماء أو تردى من الجبل لا يحرم لأن موته قد حصل بالإبانة حكماً فلا يضاف إلى غيره، وإن كان حصل بذلك حقيقة أقول : المقدمة القائلة أن المطلق ينصرف إلى الكامل شائعة في ألسنة الفقهاء وكتب أصحابنا لكنها مخالفة في الظاهر لما تقرر في أصول أن المطلق يجري على اطلاقه كما أن المقيد يجري على تقييده فتأمل في التوفيق. وفي الأصل: رجل أرسل كلبه على صيد فأخطأ ثم عرض له صيد آخر فقتله يؤكل، وإن فاته الصيد فرجع وعرض له صيد آخر في رجوعه فقتله لا يؤكل. وقوله «أبين بالذكاة» قلنا حال وقوعه لم تقع ذكاة لقيام الحياة في الثاني حقيقة وحكماً على ما بينا، وإنما تقع ذكاة عند موته وفي ذلك الوقت لا يظهر في المبان لعدم الحياة فيه لزواله بالانفصال فصار الأصل فيه أن المبان من الحي حقيقة وحكماً لا يجوز والمبان من الحي صورة لا حكماً بدليل ما ذكرنا من الأحكام من أنه لا يؤثر فيه وقوعه في النهر في هذه الحالة يحل أكله في هذه الحالة وإن كان يكره لما فيها من زيادة الإيلام بقطع لحمه ولا كذلك المبان منه بالاصطياد لأنه حقيقة وحكماً لا يثبت له حتى هذه الأحكام.
قال رحمه الله : (وإن قطعه ثلاثاً والأكثر مما يلي العجز أكل كله لأن المبان منه صورة لا حكماً، إذ لا يتوهم سلامته وبقاؤه حياً بعد هذه الجراحة فوقع ذكاة في الحال فحل
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'If he shoots game and cuts off a limb, the game is eaten, not the limb.' And al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, said: It is eaten if the game dies from it, because it is separated by the dhabh of necessity, so it is halal like that separated by dhabh of choice, unlike when it does not die because it was not separated by dhabh. Our proof is the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Whatever is cut from a living animal, then what is cut from it is carrion' narrated by Ibn Majah. He mentioned 'living' absolutely, so it applies to the living in reality and in legal ruling, the limb separated in this description because that which is separated from it is living in reality due to the existence of life in it, and likewise in legal ruling because this is its safety after the wound, and for this reason this amount of life is considered, such that if it falls into water while it has this amount of life, it is forbidden, unlike when it is separated by dhabh of choice because that which is separated from it is dead in legal ruling. Do you not see that if it falls in this state into water or falls from a mountain, it is not forbidden, because its death has occurred by the separation in legal ruling, so it is not attributed to something else, even if it occurs by that in reality. I say: The premise that the absolute applies to the perfect is widespread on the tongues of the jurists and in the books of our associates, but it is apparently contrary to what is established in usul that the absolute runs on its absolute, just as the restricted runs on its restriction. So reflect on reconciliation. And in al-Asl: A man sends his dog at game and it misses, then another game appears to it and it kills it: it is eaten. But if the game escapes it and it returns, and another game appears to it on its return and it kills it, it is not eaten. And his saying 'separated by dhabh' we said: at the time of its occurrence, no dhabh occurred, because life in the second [game] is established, in reality and in legal ruling as we have explained. Dhabh only occurs at its death, and at that time it does not appear in the separated part due to the absence of life in it because it has vanished by separation. So the principle became that the part separated from a living animal in reality and in legal ruling is not permissible, and the part separated from an animal in form but not in legal ruling is permissible, by the evidence we mentioned of the rulings: that falling into a river in this state does not affect it - it is halal to eat in this state, although it is disliked because of the increased pain by cutting its flesh; and not so the part separated by hunting, because in reality and in legal ruling it does not have even these rulings.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And if he cuts it three times or more from the side of the rump, the whole is eaten, because the separated part is in form only, not in legal ruling, since its safety and remaining alive after this wound is not conceivable, so dhabh occurred immediately, so it is halal.'
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage covers two connected rulings: (1) the cut limb from a living game animal is not halal (imam Shafi'i disagrees), and (2) if three or more cuts are made from the hind part, the entire animal becomes halal. The Hanafi proof for (1) is the hadith: 'whatever is cut from a living animal is carrion.' The commentary explains that the animal is alive both literally and legally at the moment of cutting, so the separated limb is carrion. The Shafi'i view is that if the animal dies from the cut, the limb is halal because it is considered a valid slaughter under duress (dhabh al-idtirar). The Hanafi responds that the life remains until death, and the cut does not constitute dhabh because the animal is still alive. A secondary point: if the animal (with the wound) falls into water, it becomes haram because the water may cause death, but if it falls after the limb is cut by proper dhabh, it is not haram. Then a qawida about absolute and restricted is noted but not resolved. An example from al-Asl about a hunting dog is included. For (2): if three or more cuts are made from the rump side, the animal is considered dead from the cuts immediately, so the whole animal is halal because the separation is only in form, not in legal ruling.

Key Terms
ذكاة الاضطرار (dhabh al-idtirar): An emergency form of slaughter where the animal is killed by a wound in hunting, considered valid for eating.
حي حقيقة وحكماً (hayy haqiqatan wa hukman): Alive both in literal reality (the flesh is still living) and in legal ruling (the animal is considered alive for the purposes of hunting law).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi evidence that a limb cut from a living game animal is carrion?
A) The hadith: 'Whatever is cut from a living animal is carrion'
B) The consensus of the Sahaba
C) Qiyas on slaughter
D) The principle of istihsan

Key Takeaway
A limb cut from a living animal is carrion; only if the animal is effectively dead from the outset (three cuts from rump) is the whole halal.

Ruling When First Hunter Wounds and Second Kills
Explains ownership and permissibility when two hunters hit the same game

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game hit by two hunters and apply the key concept of ḥayz al-imtināʿ to determine ownership and permissibility.

Core Idea
When the first hunter disables the game, he owns it; the second's kill makes it ḥarām for him.

Hunting in the Hanafi school involves precise rules on ownership and permissibility, especially when two hunters target the same game. This lesson examines the scenario where the first hunter wounds but does not kill, and the second delivers the fatal blow. Through the concept of ḥayz al-imtināʿ (state of unattainability), we will derive the rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Determine whether the first hunter's shot disabled the game (athkhanahu) or not. The ruling changes accordingly.
Disabling means the animal can no longer escape or resist, leaving the state of being unattainable.
2
Analyze the state of the game
If the first did not disable, the game remains in ḥayz al-imtināʿ; its slaughter is ḍhakāt al-iḍṭirār (emergency slaughter) and the second's kill is valid.
3
Apply the hadith proof
The hadith 'al-ṣayd li-man akhadhah' (the game belongs to the one who takes it) is cited. 'Taking' includes disabling, so the first acquires ownership if he disables.
The hadith is used by both sides depending on the context.
4
Distinguish the intermediate case
When the first wound leaves the game with life above that of a slaughtered animal (e.g., could live a day), the school is divided: Abu Yusuf holds the second's kill does not make it ḥarām; Muḥammad holds it does.
This ikhtilāf is a legitimate difference within the Ḥanafī school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحل وإن أثخنه فللأول وحرم وضمن الثاني للأول قيمته غير ما نقصته لأنه كتاب الصيد جراحته . أكله كما إذا أبين رأسه في الذكاة الاختيارية، وكذا إذا قد نصفين لما ذكرنا بخلاف ما إذا قطع يداً أو رجلاً أو فخذاً أو ثلثه مما يلي القوائم أو أقل من نصف الرأس حيث يحرم المبان ويحل المبان منه لأنه يتوهم بقاء الحياة في الباقي، وإن ضرب عنق شاة فأبان رأسها تحل لقطع الأوداج ويكره لما فيه من زيادة الألم بإبلاغه النخاع، وإن ضربها من قبل القفا إن ماتت قبل قطع الأوداج لا تحل وإن لم تمت حتى قطع الأوداج حلت ولو ضرب صيداً فقطع يده أو رجله ولم ينفصل حتى مات إن كان يتوهم التئامه واندماله حل أكله لأنه بمنزلة سائر أجزائه، وإن كان لا يتوهم بأن يبقى معلقاً بجلده حل ما سواه دونه لوجود الإبانة معنى والعبرة للمعاني. قال رحمه الله : وحرم صيد المجوسي والوثني والمرتد لأنهم ليسوا من أهل الذكاة حالة الاختيار فكذا حالة الاضطرار وكذا المحرم لأنه ليس من أهل ذكاة الاختيار في حق الصيد فلا يكون من أهل ذكاة الاضطرار فيه ويؤكل صيد الكتابي من أهل الذكاة اختياراً فكذا اضطراراً. قال رحمه الله: (وإن رمى صيداً فلم يثخنه فرماه الثاني فقتله فهو للثاني وحل) لأنه هو الآخد له وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام الصيد لمن أخذه وإنما حل لأنه لما لم يخرج بالأول من حيز الامتناع كان ذكاته ذكاة الاضطرار وهو الجرح في أي موضع كان وقد وجد. قال رحمه الله : (وإن أثخنه فللأول وحرم لأنه لما أثخنه الأول قد خرج من حيز الامتناع صار قادراً على ذكاته الاختيارية فوجب عليه ذكاته لما روينا ولم يذكه وصار الثاني قاتلاً له فيحرم وهو لو ترك ذكاته مع القدرة عليه يحرم فبالقتل أولى أن يحرم بخلاف الوجه الأول. وهذا إذا كان بحال يسلم من الأول لأن موته يضاف إلى الثاني أما إذا كان الرمي الأول بحال لا منه الصيد بأن لا يبقى فيه من الحياة إلا بقدر ما يبقى من المذبوح كما إذا أبان رأسه يحل لأن موته لا يضاف إلى الرمي الثاني فلا اعتبار لوجوده لكونه ميتاً حكماً، ولهذا لو وقع في الماء في هذه الحالة لا يحرم كوقوعه بعد موته ولو كان الرمي الأول بحال لا يعيش به الصيد لكن حياته فوق حياة المذبوح بأن كان يبقى يوماً أو دونه فعند أبي يوسف لا يحرم بالرمية الثانية لأن هذا القدر من الحياة لا يعتبر عنده وعند محمد يحرم لأن هذا القدر من الحياة يعتبر عنده فصار حكمه كحكم ما إذا كان الأول يسلم منه فلا يحل
And it is permissible. If he disabled it, then it is for the first, and it is forbidden, and the second must guarantee to the first its value minus what his wound decreased. Its eating is like when its head is severed in voluntary slaughter, and likewise if he cuts it in two halves for what we mentioned, contrary to if he cuts a hand, leg, thigh, or a third of it from the side of the legs, or less than half of the head, where the severed part is forbidden and the remaining part is permissible because the survival of life in the remainder is possible. And if he struck the neck of a sheep and severed its head, it is permissible because the jugular veins are cut, but it is disliked because of the increased pain by reaching the spinal cord. And if he struck it from the back of the neck, if it died before cutting the jugular veins, it is not permissible; if it did not die until the jugular veins were cut, it is permissible. And if he struck game and cut its hand or leg and it did not separate until it died, if the healing and closing of the wound is possible, its eating is permissible because it is like the rest of its parts. And if it is not possible, and it remains hanging by its skin, what is other than it is permissible, but it is not, due to the existence of severance in meaning, and consideration is for meanings. He (the author) said: And the game of a Magian, idolater, and apostate is forbidden because they are not among the people of slaughter in the voluntary case, so likewise in the case of necessity. Likewise the muhrim (in ihram) because he is not among the people of voluntary slaughter regarding game, so he cannot be among the people of necessity slaughter in it. And the game of a Kitabi (People of the Book) is eaten because he is among the people of slaughter voluntarily, so likewise by necessity. He (the author) said: (And if he shot game and did not disable it, then the second shot and killed it, then it is for the second and is permissible) because he is the one who took it, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Game belongs to the one who takes it.' And it is only permissible because when the first did not remove it from the state of being unattainable, its slaughter is the slaughter of necessity, which is the wound in any place, and it has occurred. He (the author) said: (And if he disabled it, then it is for the first and is forbidden) because when the first disabled it, it has left the state of being unattainable, and he became able to perform voluntary slaughter, so it was obligatory for him to slaughter it as we have narrated, and he did not slaughter it, and the second became a killer of it, so it is forbidden. And if he leaves its slaughter with the ability, it is forbidden, so by killing it is more deserving of being forbidden, contrary to the first case. This is if it is in a condition that it is safe from the first, because its death is attributed to the second. But if the first shot is in a condition that does not save the game, such that no more life remains in it than what remains in a slaughtered animal, as if he severed its head, it is permissible because its death is not attributed to the second shot, so no consideration is given to its existence because it is dead by legal fiction. Therefore, if it fell into water in this state, it is not forbidden, like its falling after its death. And if the first shot is in a condition that the game cannot live with it, but its life is above the life of a slaughtered animal, such that it would remain a day or less, then according to Abu Yusuf, it is not forbidden by the second shot because this amount of life is not considered by him. According to Muhammad, it is forbidden because this amount of life is considered by him, so its ruling becomes like the ruling when the first is safe from it, so it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the laws of hunting (صيد) when two hunters strike the same game. It begins by stating the core ruling: if the first hunter wounds the game in a disabling manner (أثخنه), the game belongs to the first hunter and is unlawful (حرام) for the second; moreover, the second hunter must compensate the first for the value of the game minus the reduction caused by the first's wound. The author then elaborates with examples about severed parts and rulings on various cuts, emphasizing that the life of the game is a determining factor. After a brief digression on who is qualified to slaughter (including the ruling on Magians, idolaters, apostates, muhrim, and Kitabis), the text returns to the scenario of two hunters. The first scenario (the second kills after the first failed to disable) is addressed: the game belongs to the second and is permissible, based on the hadith 'الصيد لمن أخذه' (the game belongs to the one who takes it) and the principle that the game remained in a state of being unattainable (حيز الامتناع). The second scenario (the first disables) is then discussed: the game becomes the first's property because it has left the state of being unattainable and the first is obligated to perform voluntary slaughter; the second's killing makes him a transgressor and the game haram for him. A key exception follows: if the first wound is so severe that the game has no more life than a slaughtered animal (e.g., head severed), then the second's shot is irrelevant and the game is permissible because its death is not attributed to the second. An intermediate case arises when the first wound leaves the game with life above that of a slaughtered animal (e.g., could live a day or less) but still not survivable. Here, Abu Yusuf holds that the second's shot does not make it haram (i.e., it remains permissible) because such residual life is inconsequential, while Muhammad holds it becomes haram because the life is significant. This intra-Hanafi dispute shows the precision with which the school weighs the degree of remaining life in hunting scenarios.

Key Terms
أثخنه (athkhanahu): To wound severely, disabling the animal so it cannot escape or resist; often used for the state where the hunter becomes capable of performing voluntary slaughter.
ذكاة الاضطرار (dhakāt al-iḍtirār): Emergency slaughter by wounding, applicable when the animal cannot be slaughtered in the normal manner because it is still wild or escaping; the wound itself serves as the slaughter.
حيز الامتناع (ḥayz al-imtinā'): The state of being unattainable or resistant to capture; the animal is still capable of fleeing or defending itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the first hunter wounds the game severely (أثخنه) and then the second hunter kills it, what is the ruling on the game for the second hunter?
A) It is halal for the second hunter because he killed it.
B) It is haram for the second hunter, and he must pay the value to the first.
C) It belongs to the first hunter but is halal for the second if he gives compensation.
D) It is permissible for both hunters to share.

Key Takeaway
The ownership and permissibility of game in a two-hunter scenario hinge on whether the first hunter disabled the animal, transferring it out of the state of being unattainable (حيز الامتناع).

The Second Hunter's Guarantee for Disabled Game
Explains compensation owed by second hunter to first in various scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the compensation owed by a second hunter when he kills game already wounded by the first, applying the Hanafi rules of daman.

Core Idea
The second hunter's compensation equals the value at death minus the first wound's reduction; if both wounds contributed, a breakdown ensures no double payment.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into a specific hunting scenario: when a second hunter kills game that the first had disabled. The ruling clarifies that the second's liability is not the full healthy value but the value at the time of destruction, reflecting the prior wound. The text presents two methods—a simple total and a detailed breakdown from al-Ziyadat—and shows they produce the same compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is the second hunter killing game already wounded by the first. The ruling (hukm) is that he must pay daman (guarantee) to the first owner.
The first hunter becomes owner by disabling the game (ithkhan).
2
Determine the Basis for Liability
The second destroyed property owned by another. The value is assessed at the time of destruction, which includes the prior wound.
This is analogous to destroying a sick slave or wounded sheep.
3
Contrast the Two Scenarios
If only the second caused death, he pays the full value minus the decrease from the first wound. If both wounds contributed or cause unknown, a three-part breakdown from al-Ziyadat applies.
The text confirms both methods yield the same total.
4
Walk Through the Numerical Example
Game worth 10 dirhams, first wound decreases by 2, second wound decreases by 2, then death. First method: second pays 8. Second method: pay 2 (wound decrease) + half of remaining 6 (3) as live value + after death another 3 = total 8.
The meat value is included in the live value, so no double payment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : وضمن الثاني للأول قيمته غير ما نقصته جراحته) أي ضمن جميع قيمة الصيد غير ما نقصته جراحته الأولى لأنه أتلف صيداً مملوكاً للغير لأنه ملكه بالإثخان فيلزم قيمة ما أتلفه وقيمته وقت إتلافه كان ناقصاً بجراحة الأول فيلزم ذلك لأن الملتف تعتبر وقت الإتلاف فصار كما لو أتلف عبداً مريضاً أو شاة مجروحة فإنه يلزمه قيمته متقوماً بالمرض أو الجرح. وقال صاحب الهداية وغيره : تأويله إذا علم أن القتل حصل بالثاني، فإن كان الأول بحال يسلم منه والثاني بحال لا يسلم منه ليكون القتل كله مضافاً إلى الثاني وقد قتل حيواناً جراحته فلأنه جرح حيواناً مملوكاً كالأول منقوصاً بالجراحة فلا يضمنه كاملاً، وإن علم أن الموت حصل من الجراحتين أو لا يدري قال صاحب الهداية قال في الزيادات: يضمن الثاني ما نقصته جراحته ثم يضمن نصف قيمته مجروحاً بجراحتين ثم يضمن نصف قيمته لحماً. أما الأول وهو ما نقصته حيواناً مملوكاً للغير وقد نقصته فيضمنه أولا، وأما الثاني وهو نصف قيمته حياً فلأن الموت حصل بالجراحتين فيكون هو متلفاً نصفه وهو مملوك لغيره فيضمن نصف قيمته مجروحاً لجراحتين لأن الأولى ما كانت بصنعه يعني الجراحة الأولى ما كانت بصنع الثاني فلا يضمنها والثانية ضمنها مرة فلا يضمنها ثانية أي الجراحة الثانية ومراده ما نقص بجراحته ضمنها مرة وهو ما ضمنه من النقصان بجراحته أولاً. وأما الثالث وهو ضمان نصف اللحم فلأن بالرمية الأولى صار بحال يحل بذكاة الاختيار لولا رمي الثاني فهذا بالرمي الثاني أفسد عليه نصف اللحم فيضمنه ولا يضمن نصف القيمة لآخر لأنه ضمنه من حيث ضمن نصف قيمته حياً فدخل ضمان اللحم. وهذا المسألتين فرقاً أعني بين ما إذا حصل القتل بالثاني وحده أو بهما وليس كذلك، بل لا فرق بينهما لأنه في الموضعين يضمن الثاني جميع قيمته غير ما نقصته جراحة الأول إلا أنه بین المسألة الأولى جميع الحاصل، وفي الثانية بين طريق الضمان؛ نقل ذلك عن قاضيخان أي عدم الفرق بين المسألتين؛ بيانه أن الرامي الأول إذا رمى صيداً يساوي عشرة فنصقه در همين ثم رماه الثاني فنقصه در همين ثم مات فعلى الطريقة الأولى يضمن الثاني ثمانية ويسقط عنه قیمته دره من لأن ذلك تلف الأول وهو المراد بقوله «غير ما نقصته جراحته». وعلى الطريقة الثانية بجرح يضمن در همين أولاً لأن ذلك القدر من النقصان حصل بفعله وهو المراد بقوله في الزيادات يضمن الثاني ما نقصته جراحته بقي من قيمته ستة فيضمن نصفها وهو ثلاثة دراهم وهو المراد بقوله «ثم يضمن نصف قيمته مجروحاً بجراحتين» يعني به نصف قيمته حياً. ثم إذا مات يضمن النصف الآخر بعد الموت وإن كان تفويت اللحم فيه موجوداً بقتله لأنه ضمن ذلك النصف حياً، فلو ضمنه بعد الموت كان يتكرر الضمان بأن يضمن قيمته حياً ثم يضمن قيمته لحماً بعد الموت وهذا لا يجوز. وهذا إذا كانت حياته خفية بقدر المذبوح فلا يضمن الثاني ويوكل لأن موته لا يضاف إلى الثاني ولهدا لو وقع في الماء في هذه الحالة لا يحرم وقد ذكرناه من قبل وعنه وقع الاحتراز بقوله فإن علم أن الموت حصل من الجراحتين أو لا يدري
He (the author) said: And the second guarantees to the first its value minus what his wound decreased, meaning he guarantees the entire value of the game minus what the first wound decreased, because he destroyed a game owned by someone else because he owned it by disabling it, so he must pay the value of what he destroyed, and its value at the time of destruction was reduced by the first wound, so he must pay that because the compensation is considered at the time of destruction. So it is as if he destroyed a sick slave or a wounded sheep, then he must pay its value valued by the illness or wound. The author of al-Hidayah and others said: Its interpretation is if it is known that the killing happened by the second, and if the first is in a condition that he is safe from it, and the second in a condition that he is not safe from it, so that the entire killing is attributed to the second, and he killed an animal, its wound because he wounded a owned animal like the first, deficient by the wound, so he does not guarantee it fully. And if it is known that death happened from both wounds or it is not known, the author of al-Hidayah said in al-Ziyadat: The second guarantees what his wound decreased, then he guarantees half of its value wounded by two wounds, then he guarantees half of its value as meat. As for the first, which is what his wound decreased, he is a owned animal belonging to another and he decreased it, so he guarantees it first. As for the second, which is half of its value alive, because death occurred from both wounds, so he is a destroyer of half of it and it is owned by another, so he guarantees half of its value wounded by two wounds because the first was not his action, meaning the first wound was not by the second's action, so he does not guarantee it, and the second he guaranteed once, so he does not guarantee it a second time, meaning the second wound, and what he intended is the decrease from his wound; he guaranteed it once, which is what he guaranteed from the decrease by his wound first. As for the third, which is the guarantee of half the meat, because by the first shot it became in a condition that it would be permissible by voluntary slaughter if not for the second shot, so this by the second shot spoiled half the meat for him, so he guarantees it. And he does not guarantee half the value of the meat again because he already guaranteed it by guaranteeing half of its value alive, so the guarantee of meat is included. And these two cases differ, meaning between when death occurred by the second alone or by both, but it is not so; rather there is no difference between them because in both places the second guarantees the entire value minus what the first wound decreased, except that in the first case he explains the whole outcome, and in the second he explains the method of guarantee. This is transmitted from Qadi Khan, meaning no difference between the two cases. Its explanation: If the first shooter shot a game worth ten, and it decreased by two dirhams, then the second shot and decreased it by two dirhams, then it died. According to the first method, the second guarantees eight and drops from him its value of a dirham? Because that is the loss of the first, and that is meant by his statement 'other than what his wound decreased'. According to the second method, by wound he guarantees two dirhams first, because that amount of decrease occurred by his action, and that is meant by his statement in al-Ziyadat: the second guarantees what his wound decreased. The remainder of its value is six, so he guarantees half of it, which is three dirhams, and that is meant by his statement 'then he guarantees half of its value wounded by two wounds' meaning half of its value alive. Then when it dies, he guarantees the other half after death, although the spoiling of meat is present in its killing, because he already guaranteed that half alive. If he guaranteed it after death, the guarantee would be repeated – he would guarantee its value alive then its value as meat after death – and that is not permissible. And this is if its life is hidden to the extent of a slaughtered animal, then the second does not guarantee and it is eaten, because its death is not attributed to the second; therefore, if it fell into water in this state it is not forbidden, and we have mentioned it before. And from him came the caution with his statement: so if it is known that death occurred from both wounds or it is not known.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the guarantee (ضمان) owed by the second hunter to the first when the second kills game that the first had disabled. The author begins by clarifying the phrase 'قيمته غير ما نقصته جراحته' – the second must pay the full value of the game at the time of destruction, but that value is already reduced by the first's wound. This is analogous to destroying a sick slave or a wounded sheep – the liability is for the diminished value. The author of al-Hidayah adds that this applies when it is known that the killing was solely by the second; if death resulted from both wounds or is unknown, a different rule from al-Ziyadat applies. That rule has three components: (1) the second pays for the decrease caused by his own wound, (2) then pays half the value of the animal while alive with both wounds (since each wound contributed equally to death, he is liable for half the animal's value as a living thing), and (3) then pays half the value of the meat (because the second shot prevented the first from performing voluntary slaughter, which would have made the meat permissible). The text emphasizes that this is not an additional double payment but a breakdown of the total compensation. A numerical example illustrates: game worth 10, first wound reduces by 2, second wound reduces by 2. By the first method, second pays 8. By the second method: second pays 2 for his wound decrease, then of the remaining 6, he pays half (3) as half the alive value, and then after death he doesn't pay again because the meat value is already covered by the half alive value. The passage clarifies there is no practical difference between the two methods in total amount – both result in the same compensation – but the second method explains the breakdown. The text also reiterates the earlier exception: if the first wound left the animal with life only equal to that of a slaughtered animal, the second owes nothing and the game is permissible, as death is not attributed to him.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Financial liability or guarantee; the obligation to compensate for loss or damage to another's property.
الزادات (al-Ziyādāt): One of the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, containing additional rulings and details.
حياً (ḥayyan): Alive; used in the context of valuing the animal while it was still alive before death, as opposed to its value as meat after death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the method from al-Ziyadat, if the first hunter's wound reduces the game's value by 2 dirhams and the second's wound reduces it by 2 dirhams, and the game dies from both wounds (initial value 10), how much does the second hunter pay in total?
A) 2 dirhams
B) 5 dirhams
C) 8 dirhams
D) 10 dirhams

Key Takeaway
The second hunter's compensation always equals the value at the time of his shot minus the first wound's reduction, regardless of whether he acted alone or with the first; the detailed breakdown merely clarifies the components.
