Ownership of Game Between Two Hunters: The Rule of First Disabling
Resolves ownership when two hunters shoot at the same game with overlapping wounds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of first disabling (athkhana) to determine ownership of game shot by two hunters in overlapping sequences, and explain Zafar's dissenting view and its rebuttal.

Core Idea
Ownership belongs to the hunter who disables the game first—not who hits first or kills it.

When two hunters shoot the same animal, timing matters. The Hanafi school rules that the hunter whose shot first disables the animal (athkhanahu) becomes its owner, even if the other's arrow arrives later or kills. Zafar, a Hanafi scholar, disagreed when the second's shot hits before the first disables. This lesson unpacks the scenarios and the legal reasoning behind the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core Hanafi rule
The text repeatedly states: the game belongs to the first hunter who disables it (أثخنه). Disabling means the animal can no longer escape. Note that ownership is not based on who hits first or who kills, but on who disables.
2
List the three overlapping scenarios
The passage covers: (1) simultaneous shots but one hits and disables before the other; (2) first shoots, second shoots before first's arrow hits; (3) first hits but does not disable, then second hits and kills. In all cases, if the first eventually disables before the second's fatal hit, the first owns.
3
Understand Zafar's objection
Zafar argues that if the second's arrow hits when the animal is not yet disabled (i.e., still unattainable/mumtani'), that hit counts as a valid emergency slaughter (dhakāt al-iḍtirār), making the animal halal for the second. He analogizes the second's shot to a normal hunt where the hunter's own shot is the slaughter.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi reply: at the moment of the second's shot, the animal is still unattainable (mumtani'); thus his shot is indeed a slaughter. But the first disabling earlier created ownership, so the second's slaughter is invalid for him—it is a transgression. The animal belongs to the first, who must then perform proper slaughter.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Zafar's view, if the second's arrow kills before the first disables, the second may own and eat it. Under the Hanafi position, the first's disabling (if it occurs before the second's fatal wound) always gives the first ownership, making the second's kill haram for him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رمياه معاً فأصابه أحدهما قبل الآخر فأثخنه ثم أصابه الآخر أو رماه أحدهما أولاً ثم رماه الثاني قبل أن يصيبه الأول أو بعد ما أصابه قبل أن يثخنه فأصابه الأول فأثخنه أو أثخنه ثم أصابه الثاني فقتله فهو للأول ويؤكل. وقال زفر : يحل أكله لأنه حال إصابة الثاني غير ممتنع فلا يحل بذكاة الاضطرار فصار كما إذا رماه الثاني بعد ما أثخنه الأول. قلنا: عند رمي الثاني هو صيد ممتنع فوقع رميه ذكاة ولهذا تشترط التسمية عند فكذا الامتناع يعتبر الرمي
And if they both shot together, and one of them hit it before the other and disabled it, then the other hit it, or one shot it first then the second shot it before the first hit it, or after he hit it before he disabled it, then the first hit it and disabled it, or he disabled it then the second hit it and killed it, then it is for the first and is eaten. Zafar said: Its eating is permissible because at the time of the second's hitting it was not unattainable, so it cannot be made permissible by emergency slaughter. So it is as if the second shot it after the first disabled it. We said: At the time of the second's shot, it is a game that is unattainable, so his shot falls as a slaughter, and therefore the tasmiyah is required at the time of shooting; likewise the state of being unattainable is considered at the time of shooting.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage deals with complex cases of two hunters shooting the same game, where the timing of hits and disabling matters. The author presents the Hanafi position: regardless of the sequence, if the first hunter's shot disables the game (أثخنه) before the second's shot, the game belongs to the first and is halal (the first must slaughter it properly). This includes scenarios where the second shot hits before the first shot arrives but the first subsequently disables, etc. The key is that the game becomes owned by the first at the moment of disabling. The text then mentions Zafar's dissenting opinion: he argues that if the second's arrow hits the game when it is not yet unattainable (i.e., before the first's arrow has disabled it), then the second's shot should be considered a valid emergency slaughter, making the game halal for the taker. The Hanafi response is that at the moment of the second's shot, the game is still unattainable (ممتنع) because the first's arrow has not yet hit or disabled it; therefore, the second's shot can indeed be a cause. However, the text counters that the second's shot only becomes effective if the game is not already virtually owned? Actually the reply is somewhat unclear due to the abrupt end. The standard Hanafi view is that the first to disable owns it, so if the second kills after the first disabled, it is haram for the second. If the second kills before the first disables, the second owns it. The passage mentions various sequences and ends with a rebuttal to Zafar.

Key Terms
أثخنه (athkhanahu): To wound severely, disabling the animal; the moment the animal becomes unable to escape.
ممتنع (mumtani'): Unable to be caught or easily subdued; the game is still in a state of wildness or escape.
ذكاة اضطرار (dhakāt al-iḍtirār): Emergency slaughter by wounding, applicable when the animal is still ممتنع and cannot be slaughtered normally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two hunters shoot at a gazelle. Hunter X's arrow hits first but only slightly wounds the gazelle (it can still run). Hunter Y's arrow then hits and kills it. Later, Hunter X's second arrow hits the already dead gazelle. According to the Hanafi school, who owns the gazelle?
A) Hunter X, because his first arrow hit first.
B) Hunter Y, because his arrow killed the gazelle.
C) Neither, the gazelle is carrion.
D) Hunter X, because his second arrow disabled the gazelle.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ownership of game is determined by the first disabling (athkhana), not by the first hit or the kill.

Liability of the Second Hunter in Combined Wounds
Explains liability when two hunters wound an animal and it dies from combined wounds or unknown cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the compensation owed by the second hunter when two hunters wound an animal and it dies from combined wounds, stating the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Second hunter pays full value minus first wound reduction—same whether he kills alone or jointly.

When two hunters wound an animal and it dies, the question arises: who pays what? The Hanafi school provides a detailed ruling that ensures fairness while avoiding double compensation. This lesson unpacks the reasoning from Al-Hidayah and Al-Ziyadat, explaining the calculation and resolving an apparent intra-madhhab dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Determine the hukm level: it is wajib for the second hunter to compensate for his damage and half the animal's value if death results from both wounds.
The ruling is based on principles of property damage (daman).
2
List the three compensation components from al-Ziyadat
First: what his wound diminished. Second: half its value alive (as if wounded by two wounds). Third: half its value as meat.
These are explained sequentially in the text.
3
Understand the reasoning for each component
First: he caused that reduction. Second: death from both means he destroyed half, but excludes the first wound's damage. Third: second shot spoiled the meat that would have been permissible by slaughter.
Avoiding double compensation is key.
4
Resolve the apparent difference between the two scenarios
The text initially distinguishes when death is from second alone vs both, but Qadikhan clarifies the total is the same: full value minus first hunter's reduction.
The difference is only in explaining how the compensation is broken down.
5
Apply the numerical example
Work through the example: animal worth 10 dirhams, first reduces by 2, second by 2. Second pays 8 dirhams total.
Verify that both methods yield 8 dirhams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جر راحته فلأنه جرح حيوا يوهم أن بين ضمان همان مملوكاً كالأول منقوصاً بالجراحة فلا يضمنه كاملاً، وإن علم أن الموت حصل من الجراحتين أو لا يدري قال صاحب الهداية قال في الزيادات: يضمن الثاني ما نقصته جراحته ثم يضمن نصف قيمته مجروحاً بجراحتين ثم يضمن نصف قيمته لحماً. أما الأول وهو ما نقصته حيواناً مملوكاً للغير وقد نقصته فيضمنه أولا، وأما الثاني وهو نصف قيمته حياً فلأن الموت حصل بالجراحتين فيكون هو يضمن الثاني جميع قيمته غير ما نقصته جراحة الأول إلا أنه بین المسألة الأولى جميع الحاصل، وفي الثانية بين طريق الضمان؛ نقل ذلك عن قاضيخان أي عدم الفرق بين المسألتين؛ بيانه أن الرامي الأول إذا رمى صيداً يساوي عشرة فنصقه در همين ثم رماه الثاني فنقصه در همين ثم مات فعلى الطريق الأولى يضمن الثاني ثمانية ويسقط عنه قیمته دره من لأن ذلك تلف الأول وهو المراد بقوله «غير ما نقصته جراحته». وعلى الطريقة الثانية بجرح يضمن در همين أولاً لأن ذلك القدر من النقصان حصل بفعله وهو المراد بقوله في الزيادات يضمن الثاني ما نقصته جراحته بقي من قيمته ستة فيضمن نصفها وهو ثلاثة دراهم وهو المراد بقوله «ثم يضمن نصف قيمته مجروحاً بجراحتين» يعني به نصف قيمته حياً. ثم إذا مات يضمن النصف الآخر بعد الموت وإن كان تفويت اللحم فيه موجوداً بقتله لأنه ضمن ذلك النصف حياً، فلو ضمنه بعد الموت كان يتكرر الضمان بأن يضمن قيمته حياً وقع الاحتراز بقوله فإن علم أن الموت حصل من الجراحتين أو لا يدري».
His comfort [؟] because it wounds a living creature, suggesting that there is compensation [؟] owned like the first, diminished by the wound, so he does not compensate it fully. And if it is known that death occurred from both wounds or it is not known, the author of al-Hidayah said, it is stated in al-Ziyadat: The second compensates what his wound diminished, then he compensates half its value wounded by two wounds, then he compensates half its value as meat. As for the first, which is what his wound diminished from an animal owned by another and he diminished it, so he compensates it first. As for the second, which is half its value alive, because death occurred from both wounds, so he is the destroyer of half of it and it is owned by another, so he compensates half its value wounded by two wounds because the first (wound) was not by his action, meaning the first wound was not by the action of the second, so he does not compensate it, and the second he compensated once so he does not compensate it again, i.e., the second wound. And his meaning is that what was diminished by his wound he compensated once, which is what he compensated of the reduction by his wound first. As for the third, which is compensation of half the meat, because by the first shot it became in a state that it would be permissible by slaughter of choice were it not for the second shot, so by the second shot he spoiled half the meat for him, so he compensates it. And he does not compensate half the value to another because he compensated it from the perspective that he compensated half its value alive, so the compensation for meat is included. And these two cases differ, meaning between when death occurs by the second alone or by both, but it is not so; rather there is no difference between them because in both cases the second compensates all its value except what the wound of the first diminished, except that he explained in the first case the total result, and in the second he explained the method of compensation. That is transmitted from Qadikhan, meaning no difference between the two cases. Explanation: If the first hunter shot an animal worth ten, and it diminished by two dirhams, then the second shot it and it diminished by two dirhams, then it died. According to the first method, the second compensates eight, and its value of a dirham [؟] is dropped because that is the loss from the first, and that is the meaning of his statement 'except what his wound diminished'. According to the second method by wound, he compensates two dirhams first because that amount of reduction occurred by his action, and that is the meaning of his statement in al-Ziyadat: the second compensates what his wound diminished. The remainder of its value is six, so he compensates half of it, which is three dirhams, and that is the meaning of his statement 'then he compensates half its value wounded by two wounds', meaning half its value alive. Then when it dies, he compensates the other half after death, even though the loss of meat is present in its killing, because he already compensated that half while alive. If he were to compensate it after death, compensation would be repeated by compensating its value alive then compensating its value as meat after death, and that is not permissible. And this is if its life was hidden like the slaughtered (animal), so the second does not compensate and he is excused because its death is not attributed to the second. Therefore, if it fell into water in this state, it is not prohibited. We have mentioned it before, and from it comes the caution with his statement: 'If it is known that death occurred from both wounds or it is not known'.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Hanafi school on liability when two hunters wound an animal and it dies from their combined wounds or the cause is uncertain. The text, expanding on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, quotes al-Hidayah and al-Ziyadat to detail the compensation owed by the second hunter. The ruling states that the second hunter first pays for the reduction in value caused by his own wound. Then, because death resulted from both wounds, he is considered to have destroyed half of the animal and thus pays half its value as if it were alive and wounded by two wounds. Finally, he pays half its value as meat because the first shot made it permissible to slaughter, but the second shot spoiled that. The text then addresses a potential misunderstanding: there is no practical difference between the case where the second hunter killed alone and the case where both contributed. In both, the second hunter pays the full value minus the reduction from the first hunter's wound. Qadikhan confirms this. A numerical example is given: if the animal is worth ten dirhams, the first wound reduces by two, the second by two, and then it dies. In the first method, the second pays eight dirhams (ten minus two). In the second method, he first pays two for his reduction, then half of the remaining six (three), totalling five? Actually the text says he pays two first, then three, total five; but then it says he would pay the other half after death, but that would be duplication. The correct view is that he pays the full value minus the first reduction, which is eight. The commentary clarifies that compensation is not duplicated: since he already paid for half the value alive, he does not pay for the meat separately. However, if the animal's life was hidden (like an animal that appears dead but still has life), the second hunter is not liable because the death cannot be attributed to him. The passage ends with a caution that the ruling applies when death is known to be from both wounds or is unknown.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability or compensation for damage or destruction of property.
جراحة (jaraha): Wound or injury inflicted on the animal by the hunter's shot.
قيمة (qima): Market value of the animal at the time of injury or death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two hunters wound an animal, the first causing a reduction of 2 dirhams and the second also causing a reduction of 2 dirhams, and the animal dies from the combined wounds, what does the second hunter pay according to the relied-upon view?
A) 8 dirhams
B) 10 dirhams
C) 2 dirhams for his reduction plus 3 dirhams for half the remaining value, totalling 5 dirhams
D) Nothing; only the first hunter pays

Key Takeaway
The second hunter's liability is the full value of the animal minus the reduction caused by the first hunter's wound, whether death results from the second alone or from both.

Ownership When Hunters Shoot Sequentially or Simultaneously
Determines ownership and permissibility when two hunters shoot at same game

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership of hunted game when two hunters shoot in sequence, and explain the distinction between permissibility (hillah) and ownership (milk) based on the time of shooting vs. time of mortal wounding.

Core Idea
Ownership of a hunted animal goes to the hunter who first inflicts a mortal wound (ithkhan), even if a second hunter kills it.

When two hunters aim at the same game, determining who owns the animal and whether its meat is halal depends on the order of events. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lays out the Hanafi view, including an intra-madhhab disagreement with Imam Zufar. The key is distinguishing between two legal moments: the act of shooting (which determines permissibility and liability) and the act of mortally wounding (which establishes ownership).

Guided Path
1
Identify the key scenarios
The passage lists several sequences: both shoot together, one shoots first then second before/after first hits, etc. Identify the pattern of events and the ruling for each.
Focus on the order of hitting and mortally wounding (ithkhan).
2
Determine the ruling for each scenario
For each sequence, state who owns the animal and whether the meat is halal according to the Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad).
The majority holds that ownership goes to the first to inflict ithkhan, and the meat is halal.
3
Examine Zufar's objection
Zufar argued that if the animal is already incapacitated, the second shot is not a valid slaughter (tadhkiya). Understand his reasoning based on the animal losing its capacity to escape (imtina').
Zufar's view is recorded but not relied upon for fatwa.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes between the time of shooting (animal still mumtani') and the time of mortally wounding. The second shot remains a valid slaughter because the animal retains the legal status of mumtani' until actual securing (ihraz).
The requirement of basmalah at the moment of shooting supports this distinction.
5
Apply the same principle to falcon/dog
The same rule applies to hunting birds and dogs: ownership is by ithkhan, not mere capture. If one falcon catches without wounding and another kills, the game belongs to the second.
This reinforces the principle that ithkhan, not capture, establishes ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رمياه معاً فأصابه أحدهما قبل الآخر فأثخنه ثم أصابه الآخر أو رماه أحدهما أولاً ثم رماه الثاني قبل أن يصيبه الأول أو بعد ما أصابه قبل أن يثخنه فأصابه الأول فأثخنه أو أثخنه ثم أصابه الثاني فقتله فهو للأول ويؤكل. وقال زفر : يحل أكله لأنه حال إصابة الثاني غير ممتنع فلا يحل بذكاة الاضطرار فصار كما إذا رماه الثاني بعد ما أثخنه الأول. قلنا: عند رمي الثاني هو صيد ممتنع فوقع رميه ذكاة ولهذا تشترط التسمية عند فكذا الامتناع يعتبر الرمي. وحل اصطياد ما يؤكل لحمه وما لا يؤكل . عنده إلا أن الملك يثبت للأول لأن سهمه أخرجه عن حيز الامتناع فملكه به قبل أن يقتل بسهم الثاني، فحاصله أن المعتبر في حق الحل والضمان وقت الرمي لأن الرمي إلى صيد مباح فلا ينعقد سبباً لوجوب الضمان فلا ينقلب موجباً بعد ذلك وهو ذكاة فيحل المصاب لأن الحل يحصل بفعله وفعله هو الرمي والإرسال فيعتبر وقته، وفي حق الملك يعتبر وقت الإثغان لأنه به يثبت الملك، وزفر يعتبر وقت الإثخان فيهما. ولو رمياه معاً وأصاباه معاً فمات منهما فهو بينهما لاستوائهما في السبب والبازي والكلب في هذا كالسهم حتى يملكه بإثخانه، ولا يعتبر إمساكه بدون الإثخان حتى لو أرسل بازيه فأمسك الصيد بمخلبه ولم يثخنه وأرسل الآخر بازيه فقتل ذلك الصيد فإن الصيد للثاني وحل لأن يد البازي الأول ليست يداً حافظة لتقام مقام يد المالك، أما القتل فهو إتلاف والبازي من أهل الإتلاف فينقل إلى صاحبه. ولو سهماً فأصاب الصيد فأثخنه ثم رماه ثانياً فقتله حرم لما بينا.
And if they both shot at it together, and one hit it before the other and mortally wounded it, then the other hit it; or one shot it first, then the second shot it before the first hit it, or after it hit it but before it mortally wounded it, then the first hit it and mortally wounded it, or mortally wounded it then the second hit it and killed it; then it belongs to the first and it is eaten. Zufar said: Its eating is permissible because at the moment the second hit it, it was not a game in its natural state (not capable of flight), so it is not permissible by necessity slaughter, so it becomes as if the second shot it after the first had mortally wounded it. We said: At the time of the second shot, it is a game that can still escape, so his shot is a valid slaughter, and therefore the basmalah is required at the time. Thus, the ability to escape is considered at the time of shooting. And it is permissible to hunt what is edible and what is not edible according to him, except that ownership is established for the first because his arrow removed it from the state of being able to escape, so he owns it by that before it is killed by the second arrow. So the summary is: with regard to permissibility and liability, the time of shooting is considered because the shot is directed at a permissible game, so it does not become a cause for liability, and it does not change afterwards; it is a slaughter, so the hit animal becomes permissible because permissibility is achieved by his action, and his action is the shooting and releasing, so its time is considered. With regard to ownership, the time of mortally wounding is considered because ownership is established by it. Zufar considers the time of mortally wounding for both. And if they both shot together and both hit it together and it died from them, then it is between them because they are equal in the cause. And the falcon and the dog in this are like the arrow: one owns it by mortally wounding it, and its holding without mortally wounding is not considered. So if one releases his falcon and it catches the game with its talons but does not mortally wound it, and another releases his falcon and kills that game, then the game belongs to the second and is permissible, because the hand of the first falcon is not a preserving hand to stand in place of the owner's hand. As for killing, it is destruction, and the falcon is capable of destruction, so it transfers to its owner. And if one arrow hits the game and mortally wounds it, then another shoots it and kills it, it is prohibited (unlawful) as we explained.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage addresses a set of scenarios involving two hunters shooting an animal in sequence or simultaneously, focusing on ownership and permissibility of the meat. The Hanafi ruling, as presented in the sharh, is that the first hunter who inflicts a mortal wound (ithkhan) owns the animal, and it is halal even if the second hunter kills it afterwards. Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) initially argued that the meat is halal because after the first wound the animal could no longer escape, so the second shot is not a valid slaughter (dhabh of necessity). However, the Hanafi response is that at the moment of the second shot, the animal is still capable of escape (mumtani'), so the second shot is a valid slaughter (tadhkiya), hence the requirement of basmalah. So the meat is halal regardless, but ownership goes to the first hunter because his shot removed the animal from the state of being wild (ihraz). The key distinction: for permissibility (hillah) and liability (daman), the time of shooting (al-ramy) is considered because it is the act of the hunter; for ownership (milk), the time of mortally wounding (al-ithkhan) is considered because that is when the animal is secured. Zufar disagreed, considering ithkhan for both. The text then gives the case of simultaneous hits: if both hit and kill together, the animal is shared equally. For hunting birds (bazi) and dogs, the same rule applies: ownership is by ithkhan, not by mere catching without wounding. If one falcon catches but does not wound, and another kills, the game belongs to the second. Finally, if an animal is already mortally wounded by the first arrow, a second arrow that kills it renders the meat haram (forbidden) because it was already owned and the second shot destroys it without valid slaughter.

Key Terms
إثخان (ithkhan): To mortally wound or incapacitate the animal so that it cannot escape or is effectively captured.
امتناع (imtina'): The ability of the game to flee or resist capture; a condition for valid hunting.
ذكاة (tadhkiya): The ritual slaughter or killing of an animal in a manner that makes its meat permissible (halal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two hunters shoot at a deer. The first hits it and it falls down, unable to move. The second then shoots and kills it. According to the Hanafi ruling, who owns the deer?
A) The first hunter
B) The second hunter
C) Both share equally
D) No one, it becomes carrion

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the ownership of a hunted animal is determined by the first hunter to inflict a mortal wound, while the meat remains halal as long as each shot was a valid slaughter at the time of shooting.

Permissibility of Hunting All Animals: Edible and Non-Edible
States the ruling that hunting all animals is permissible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hunting non-edible animals and identify the Quranic proof.

Core Idea
Hunting all animals is permissible — the Quranic command is unrestricted, and benefit from skin or repelling harm is legitimate.

This lesson examines a clear ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq: it is permissible to hunt both animals whose meat is eaten and those not eaten. The author grounds this on the absolute command in Surah al-Ma'idah and on rational benefits. We will unpack the proof and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the legal status of hunting according to the passage. Is it obligatory, recommended, permissible, or prohibited?
2
Locate the Primary Proof
Find the Quranic verse cited and note its wording. Why is this verse chosen?
The verse is Al-Ma'idah 5:2, 'And when you have completed the pilgrimage, then hunt.'
3
Analyze the Wajh al-Dalalah
Explain how the verse proves permissibility for all animals. What linguistic feature supports this?
The command is absolute (mutlaq) with no restriction to edible animals.
4
Consider Rational Arguments
List the additional benefits mentioned: using skin, feathers, hair, or repelling harm. How do these support the ruling?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (وحل اصطياد ما يؤكل لحمه وما لا يؤكل لقوله تعالى وإذا حللتم فاصطادوا [المائدة؛ ٢] مطلقاً من غير قيد بالمأكول إذا الصيد لا يختص بالمأكول . قال الشاعر : رمی صيد الملوك أرانب وتـعـالـب وإذا ركبت فـصـيـدك الأبطال ولأن الاصطياد سبب الانتفاع بجلده أو ريشه أو شعره أو لاستدفاع شره وكل ذلك مشروع والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم بالصواب.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And it is permissible to hunt what is eaten for its meat and what is not eaten, because of the statement of Allah Almighty: "And when you have completed the pilgrimage, then hunt" [Al-Ma'idah 5:2] absolutely without restriction to edible animals, since hunting is not specific to edible animals.' The poet said: 'The kings hunted rabbits and foxes, and when you ride, your prey are heroes.' And because hunting is a means of benefiting from its skin, feathers, or hair, or to repel its harm, and all that is legislated. And Allah, the Exalted, knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This is a clear ruling statement from the matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) that it is permissible to hunt all animals, whether their meat is edible or not. The proof is the general command in the Quran (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:2) to hunt after the pilgrimage, which is unrestricted (mutlaq) and does not specify edible animals. The commentary supports this with a poetic quote and rational arguments: hunting brings benefit by using the animal's skin, feathers, or hair, or by repelling its harm. All these purposes are legitimate in Islamic law. The author ends with a prayer. This passage sets the general permissibility of hunting, which is then qualified by other rules regarding trained animals, slaughter, and respective prohibitions.

Key Terms
اصطياد (istiyad): The act of hunting or capturing wild animals.
صيد (sayd): Game animals that are hunted, including those whose meat is eaten and those not eaten.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The verse 'Eat of the good things We have provided you' (Al-Baqarah 2:172)
C) The hadith 'The best game is the antelope'
D) Consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
Hunting all animals is permissible because the Quranic command is absolute and there is legitimate benefit.

Father's Pledge of Minor's Property: Rulings and Objection to the Part-Whole Maxim
Explains father's authority to pledge minor's property and commentator's objection to a legal principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each scenario of a father pledging his minor child's property and explain why the universal principle 'what is valid for each part is valid for the whole' is rejected with counterexamples.

Core Idea
A father's pledge of minor's property binds the son until debt is paid, and an overgeneralized maxim is disproved by obvious counterexamples.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents the Hanafi ruling on a father's authority to pledge his minor child's property. It covers three scenarios: the son's inability to retrieve property after majority and father's death, the son's right to recover from the father's estate if he pays the debt, and the permissibility of pledging for both debts combined. More significantly, the commentator objects to a universal principle cited from al-'Inayah, demonstrating that what holds for parts does not necessarily hold for the whole. Understanding this objection sharpens your ability to critically evaluate legal maxims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The text presents three distinct cases: (a) father pledges minor's property for his own debt and son reaches majority after father's death, (b) son pays off the debt himself, (c) father pledges for both his and the minor's debts.
The scenarios share the father's binding authority.
2
State the ruling for each
(a) Son cannot retrieve property until debt is paid. (b) Son recovers from father's estate. (c) Pledge is permissible; the father's debt share is treated as if the whole property was pledged for that debt.
The third ruling applies despite the objection to the maxim.
3
Locate the universal principle
The text cites from al-'Inayah: 'Whatever is permissible to establish for each part of a composite is permissible for the whole, but not the reverse.'
This principle is the target of the critique.
4
Analyze the commentator's objection
The author (Ibn Nujaym) gives three counterexamples: carrying house parts vs. whole, fighting individual soldiers vs. entire army, marrying sisters separately vs. together.
The examples show physical and legal impossibility.
5
Understand the residual ruling
Despite rejecting the principle, the practical ruling for the dual debt case remains: the father's share is treated as if the entire property were pledged for his debt.
This shows rulings can survive flawed reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فبلغ الابن ومات الأب فليس للابن أن يسترده حتى يقضي الدين لأن تصرف الأب عليه نافذ لازم له بمنزلة تصرفه بنفسه بعد البلوغ، ولو كان على الأب دين لرجل فرهن به مال الصغير فقضاه الابن بعد البلوغ رجع به في مال الأب لأنه مضطر إليه لحاجة الانتفاع بماله فأشبه الرهن وكذلك إذا هلك قبل أن يفتكه لأن الأب يصير قاضياً دينه به. ولو رهن الأب مال الصغير بدين على نفسه وبدين الصغير جاز لاشتماله على أمرين جائزين لأن كل ما جاز أن يثبت لكل واحد من أجزاء المركب جاز أن يثبت للكل دون العكس؛ هكذا قال في العناية. أقول : في هذه الكلية منع ظاهر ألا ترى أن إنساناً أو فرساً يطيق أن يحمل كل واحد من أجزاء البيت المركب من الأحجار والأشجار مثلاً ولا يطيق تحمل الكل فطعاً وأن رجلاً شجاعاً يطيق مقاتلة كل واحد من آحاد العسكر على الانفراد ولا يطيق مقاتلة مجموع العكسر معاً وهذا في الأمور الخارجية وأما في الأحكام الشرعية فكما أن يجوز للرجل أن يجامع كل من الأختين منفردة عن الأخرى بملك نكاح أو ملك يمين ولا يجوز أن يجامعهما معاً، ثم حكمه في حصة دين الأب كحكمه فيما لو كان كله رهناً بدين الأب، وكذلك الوصي والجد أب الأب
When the son reaches majority and the father dies, the son cannot recover it until the debt is paid, because the father's disposition over him is effective and binding, like his own disposition after majority. And if the father owed a debt to a man and pledged the minor's property for it, then the son after majority pays it off, he recovers it from the father's estate, because he is compelled to it for the need of benefiting from his property, so it resembles a pledge. Similarly if it perishes before he redeems it, because the father becomes one who pays his debt with it. And if the father pledges the minor's property for his own debt and for the minor's debt, it is permissible because it includes two permissible acts, since whatever is permissible to establish for each part of a composite is permissible for the whole, but not the reverse. Thus said in al-'Inayah. I say: This universal rule has an obvious objection. Do you not see that a man or horse can carry each individual part of a house composed of stones and wood but cannot carry the whole, and a brave man can fight each individual soldier of the army but cannot fight the entire company together? This is in external matters. As for legal rulings, similarly it is permissible for a man to have intercourse with each of two sisters separately by marriage or right hand possession, but not permissible to have intercourse with both together. Then the ruling for the share of the father's debt is like the ruling if it were all pledged for the father's debt. And similarly for the executor and the grandfather, the father's father.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses the father's authority to pledge his minor child's property. The first ruling establishes that after the son reaches majority and the father dies, the son cannot retrieve the pledged property until the debt is paid, because the father's act is binding. The second scenario involves the father pledging the minor's property for his own debt; if the son later pays that debt, he can recover from the father's estate. The third scenario combines both debts, and the author notes that such a pledge is permissible. However, the commentator (the author himself) objects to the principle stated in al-'Inayah that what is permissible for each part of a composite is permissible for the whole. He gives examples from physical and legal realms (like carrying house parts vs. whole, or marrying two sisters separately vs. together). Despite this objection, the practical ruling remains: the share of the father's debt is treated as if the entire property were pledged for it. The same applies to the executor and the paternal grandfather.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A contract of pledge where property is given as security for a debt.
ولاية (wilayah): Legal guardianship authorizing the father to manage the minor's property.
كلية (kulliyyah): A universal legal principle or maxim; here the rule that what is valid for each part is valid for the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a father pledges his minor son's property for his own debt, and after the father's death the son pays off the debt, what is the ruling?
A) The son cannot recover anything because the father's act is binding.
B) The son can recover the amount from the father's estate.
C) The son must wait until the other heirs agree.
D) The pledge becomes void upon the father's death.

Key Takeaway
The father's pledge of the minor's property is binding even after the son's majority and father's death, and legal maxims must be verified with concrete examples.

Executor's Pledge of Orphan's Property: Liability Rules
Rulings on executor's pledge, borrowing, loss, and liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability rules for an executor who pledges, borrows, or usurps an orphan's property, and apply the principle of ta'addi to determine when he is liable.

Core Idea
An executor is not liable for loss when acting for the orphan; personal use makes him liable.

This lesson explores the Hanafi rulings on an executor's management of orphan's property, particularly when he pledges it for a debt and subsequently uses it. The key distinction is whether the action benefits the orphan or the executor himself, determining liability (daman). Understanding these rules protects both the orphan's estate and the executor's good faith efforts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial scenario
The executor pledges orphan's goods for a debt he incurred on the orphan's behalf. Recognize the validity of this pledge and the mortgagee's possession.
The executor acts as a guardian with authority similar to a father.
2
Distinguish between borrowing for orphan's need vs. personal need
If the executor borrows the pledged item for the orphan's benefit, he is not a muta'addi (transgressor); if for his own need, he is liable.
The critical factor is the presence of wilayah (authority) for the minor's benefit.
3
Analyze loss scenarios
When the item is lost in the executor's hand, determine the effect: loss from orphan's wealth if for orphan's need; executor guarantees if for personal use.
Loss does not cancel the mortgagee's debt; the executor continues to claim for the orphan.
4
Understand usurpation (ghasb) and its consequences
If the executor usurps the pledged item and uses it for himself, he guarantees its value. The debt is settled first, surplus goes to the orphan.
Executor's guarantee is limited to the extent of his transgression.
5
Apply the principle to deferred debts and recoupment
For a deferred debt, the value becomes a pledge. If the executor acts for the orphan, he recoups from the orphan after settling with mortgagee.
The executor has authority to take orphan's property, so no usurpation against the orphan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رهن الوصي متاعاً لليتيم في دين استدانه عليه وقبضه المرتهن ثم استعاره الوصي لحاجة اليتيم فضاع في يد الوصي هلك من مال اليتيم لأن فعل الوصي كفعله بنفسه بعد البلوغ لأنه استعار لحاجة الصغير فلا يكون متعدياً بذلك، ولو هلك الرهن في يد الوصي لا يسقط من الدين شيء لخروجه عن ضمان المرتهن بالاسترداد والوصي هو الذي يطالب له على ما كان ولو استعاره الحاجة نفسه ضمنه للصغير لأنه متعد فيه لعدم ولاية الاستعمال في حاجة نفسه. ولو غصبه الوصي بعد ما رهنه فاستعمله في حاجة نفسه هلك عنده ضمن قيمته لأنه متعد في حق المرتهن بالغصب والاستعمال في حاجة نفسه فيقضي بضمان الدين، فإن فضل شيء من القدر المضمون كان لليتيم لأنه بدل ملكه، وإن لم يف بالدين يقضي من مال اليتيم لأن الدين عليه وإنما يضمن الوصي بقدر ما تعدى فيه وإن كان الدين مؤجلاً فالقيمة رهن فإذا حل كان على ما ذكرنا ولو أنه غصبه واستعمله لحاجة الصغير ضمنه لحق المرتهن لا لحق الصغير لأن استعماله في حاجة الصغير ليس يتعدد في حقه. وكذا الأخذ لأن له ولاية أخذ مال اليتيم ولهذا إذا أقر الأب أو الوصي بغصب مال الصغير لا يلزمه شيء لأنه لا يتصور غصبه لمال اليتيم لمال أن له ولاية الأخذ، فإذا هلك في يده يضمن للمرتهن فيأخذه بدينه إن كان قد حل ويرجع الوصي على الصغير لأنه ليس بمتعد في حقه بل هو عامل له، وإن كان له يحل يكون رهناً عند المرتهن . ثم إذا حل الدين يأخذه به ويرجع الوصي على الصبي لما ذكرنا
And if the executor pledges an orphan's goods for a debt he incurred on his behalf, and the mortgagee takes possession, then the executor borrows it for the orphan's need and it is lost in his hand, it is destroyed from the orphan's wealth, because the executor's act is like his own act after majority, since he borrowed for the minor's need, so he is not transgressing thereby. And if the pledge is lost in the executor's hand, nothing of the debt is waived, because it left the mortgagee's guarantee by repossession, and the executor is the one who claims for him as before. And if he borrowed it for his own need, he guarantees it to the minor because he is transgressing therein, since he has no authority to use it for his own need. And if the executor usurps it after pledging it and uses it for his own need and it perishes in his possession, he guarantees its value because he is transgressing against the mortgagee by usurpation and personal use, so the debt is settled by guarantee. If anything remains from the guaranteed amount, it belongs to the orphan, because it is the substitute for his property. And if it does not cover the debt, it is paid from the orphan's wealth because the debt is on him, and the executor only guarantees to the extent of his transgression. And if the debt is deferred, the value is a pledge; when it matures, it is as we mentioned. And if he usurps it and uses it for the minor's need, he guarantees it for the right of the mortgagee, not for the right of the minor, because his use for the minor's need is not transgression against him. Similarly, taking (the property) because he has the authority to take the orphan's property. Hence if the father or the executor admits to usurping the minor's property, nothing is binding on him, because usurpation of the orphan's property is not conceivable since he has the authority to take. So if it perishes in his hand, he guarantees to the mortgagee, and he takes it for his debt if it has matured, and the executor recoups from the minor because he is not transgressing against him but rather acting for him. And if it is not due, it remains a pledge with the mortgagee. Then when the debt matures, he takes it for it, and the executor recoups from the minor as mentioned.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage details the rulings for an executor (wasi) who pledges an orphan's property in a debt incurred for the orphan. If the executor then borrows the pledged item for the orphan's need and it is lost, the loss is from the orphan's wealth, as the executor was not transgressing. However, if he borrows it for his own need, he becomes liable to the orphan. If the executor usurps the item after pledging it and uses it for himself, he must pay its value; the debt is covered first, and any excess goes to the orphan. If the debt is insufficient, the remainder is paid from the orphan's wealth. The executor's guarantee is limited to his transgression. If he uses it for the orphan's need after usurpation, he is liable only to the mortgagee, not to the orphan, because his action benefits the orphan. The principle is that the executor has authority over the orphan's property, so his possession is not considered usurpation against the orphan. Thus, if the property perishes in his hand while in his lawful custody, he must compensate the mortgagee if the debt is due, and then he can recoup from the orphan. If the debt is not yet due, the item remains a pledge until maturity.

Key Terms
وصي (wasi): An executor appointed by the father or judge to manage the orphan's affairs.
تعد (ta'addi): Transgression or exceeding lawful bounds, incurring liability.
رهن (rahn): Pledge or mortgage; giving property as security for a debt.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee or liability; compensation for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An executor pledges an orphan's goods for a debt needed for the orphan. He then borrows the pledged item for his own personal use and it is lost. What happens?
A) The loss is from the orphan's wealth, as the executor had authority.
B) The executor must guarantee the value to the orphan and the mortgagee.
C) The mortgagee loses his right to the debt.
D) Nothing happens; the pledge continues.

Key Takeaway
An executor's liability for orphan's property hinges on whether his action benefits the orphan or himself.

Heir's Pledge and Subsequent Debt: Validity Conditions
Rulings on heir's pledge and effect of subsequent debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which an heir's pledge of inherited property is valid and explain the effect of a subsequently arising debt from a buyer's return of a defective item.

Core Idea
A pledge's validity depends on the existence of a debt at the time of pledge; a later-arising debt does not invalidate it.

This passage from al-Muhit examines the validity of an heir's pledge of inherited property when no debt is known, and the impact of a debt that arises later due to a buyer's right of return for a defect. It clarifies that timing is crucial: the debt must have existed at the moment of pledge for invalidity. The passage also addresses the pledgor's liability and the types of property valid for pledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial scenario
The heir pledges inherited property with no known debt on the deceased. The text first rules on this basic case.
2
Determine the ruling for the initial scenario
The pledge is valid because the heir could sell the property (it is saleable). The capacity to sell implies capacity to pledge.
The property must be owned and saleable.
3
Analyze the effect of a later-arising debt from defective goods return
A buyer returns a defective item sold by the deceased, creating a debt. The text holds that since the debt did not exist at the time of pledge, the pledge remains valid.
The debtor (heir) becomes guarantor for the value because the pledged property is tied up.
4
Distinguish cases where debt existed at pledge time
If a slave bought by the deceased is reclaimed (e.g., stolen), the buyer's right to the price creates a debt that retroactively existed at pledge time, invalidating the pledge.
Unknown pre-existing debts also invalidate.
5
Identify valid subjects of pledge
Gold, silver, and measurable/weighable items are valid because their value can be recovered from them.
These are 'hajarayn' (two stones) and foodstuffs/weights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حتى قال في المحيط رهن الوارث الكبير شيئاً من التركة وليس على الميت دين جاز لأنه يجوز بيعه فيجوز رهنه وإن رد عليه سلعة باعها الميت بعيب فهلكت في أيديهم ولا مال له فباعها الميت بعيب فهلكت في أيديهم ولا مال له وصح رهن الحجرين والمكيل والموزون فإن رهنت بجنسها وهلكت بمثلها من الطفلة رحمه بسبب لأن غير المرهون فالرهن جائز وصياً كان أو وارثاً، ويرجع به الوصي على اليتيم لأن الدين إنما وجب على الميت بعد الرد ولم يكن واجباً عند الرهن فصح الرهن فلا يبطل حق المرتهن لأنه للحقوق الدين في التركة بسبب الرد لكن الراهن ضامن لقيمة الرهن وجب قضاء الدين من ذلك المال ولكنه عجز عن القضاء بسبب رهنه بدينه فصار كالمتلف له فلزمه قيمته كرهن حق صاحب الدين وهو محل قضائه إلا أنه إن كان وصياً يرجع على الصغير لأنه كان عاملاً له وقد لحقه ضمان عمله. وكذلك لو زوج الميت أمته وأخذ مهرها فأعتقها الوارث بعد موته قبل الدخول بها فاختارت نفسها وصار المهر ديناً في مال الميت جاز الرهن لأن هذا الدين ثبت على الميت بعد الرهن لأنه ثبت ببطلان النكاح بعد الرهن عند الاختيار والابن ضامن له لأنه بالإعتاق أتلف حق الغريم وهو الزوج. ولو استحق عبداً ابتاعه الميت فرجع المشتري في ميراث الميت بالدين لم يجز الرهن لأنه ظهر أن الرهن وقع وعلى الميت دين ظهر أن ما قبض الميت من الثمن كان ديناً عليه للمشتري لأنه لم يجب له على المشتري مثل ذلك . قال الله : (وصح رهن الحجرين والمكيل (والموزون والمراد بالحجرين الذهب والفضة، وإنما جاز رهن هذه الأشياء لإمكان الاستيفاء منها فكانت محلاً للرهن.
Until he said in al-Muhit: The pledge of a major heir of something from the estate while there is no debt on the deceased is permissible, because it is permissible to sell it, so it is permissible to pledge it. And if there is returned to him a commodity that the deceased sold with a defect and it perishes in their hands and he has no property... So the pledge of the two stones (gold and silver) and measurable and weighable items is valid. For if it is pledged with its genus and perishes like it from the [child?]... because it is other than the pledged item, so the pledge is valid, whether the pledgor is an executor or an heir. And the executor recoups from the orphan because the debt only became due on the deceased after the return and was not due at the time of the pledge, so the pledge is valid. Thus the mortgagee's right is not invalidated because the debt is among the rights of the estate due to the return, but the pledgor is guarantor for the value of the pledge. The debt must be paid from that property, but he was unable to pay because of his pledging it for his debt, so he became like one who caused its loss, so its value is binding on him, like the pledge of the right of the debt-owner and it is the place of its payment. Except that if he is an executor, he recoups from the minor because he was acting for him and the guarantee of his action befell him. And similarly, if the deceased married off his slave-girl and took her dowry, then the heir freed her after his death before consummation, and she chose herself, and the dowry became a debt in the deceased's estate, the pledge is valid because this debt was established on the deceased after the pledge, since it was established by the annulment of the marriage after the pledge at the time of choice, and the son is guarantor for it because by freeing he destroyed the right of the creditor, who is the husband. And if a slave bought by the deceased is reclaimed, and the buyer returns to the deceased's estate with the debt, the pledge is not valid because it becomes apparent that the pledge occurred while the deceased had a debt, and it appears that what the deceased took of the price was a debt on him to the buyer, because he did not owe the buyer such a thing. Allah said: (And the pledge of the two stones and measurable and weighable items is valid), meaning gold and silver. And these items are valid for pledge because their value can be recovered from them, so they are fit for pledge.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage covers two main topics: (1) the validity of an heir's pledge of inherited property when there is no debt on the deceased, and (2) the effect of a subsequent debt arising from the return of a defective item sold by the deceased. The text from al-Muhit states that an heir can validly pledge inherited property even if there is no debt, because it can be sold. Then it discusses a scenario where a commodity sold by the deceased is returned due to a defect after the pledge; if it perishes in the hands, the ruling depends on whether the debt existed at the time of pledge. Since the debt arose after the pledge (due to the buyer's right of return), the previous pledge remains valid. However, the pledgor (heir or executor) becomes guarantor of the pledge's value because he rendered it unavailable for the debt. The executor can recoup from the orphan. Similar rules apply to freeing a slave-girl whose dowry becomes a debt. If a slave is reclaimed (stolen), the buyer's claim against the estate retroactively shows a debt existed at the time of pledge, invalidating it. Finally, the text affirms that gold, silver, and measurable/weighable items are valid subjects of pledge because their value can be realized.

Key Terms
رد بالعيب (radd bi al-ayb): The buyer's right to return a defective item to the seller.
حجرين (hajarayn): The two stones: gold and silver, used as currency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An heir pledges inherited property when there is no known debt. Later, a buyer returns a defective item sold by the deceased, creating a debt. Is the pledge valid?
A) The pledge becomes invalid because the debt now exists.
B) The pledge remains valid because the debt did not exist at the time of pledge.
C) The pledge is valid only if the heir pays the debt.
D) The pledge is valid only for the value of the defective item.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a pledge depends on the existence of a debt at the time of contracting; later-arising debts do not invalidate it, but pre-existing debts (even if unknown) do.

Dispute on Pledging Higher-Quality Goods: Zufar, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on pledge settlement when quality differs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Zufar, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding the extinguishment of a debt when a pledged ribawi item of higher quality perishes, and apply each view to a given scenario.

Core Idea
In a pledge of the same genus, quality matters differently: Zufar ignores it, Abu Yusuf offers options, Muhammad prorates.

When you pledge high-quality wheat for lower-quality wheat and it perishes, how much debt is cleared? The three great Hanafi imams disagree. This lesson walks you through their qiyas-based reasoning and the practical settlement methods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base scenario
The scenario: a good quality kurr (a measure) of food is pledged for two bad quality kurrs. The debt is two bad kurrs; the pledge is one good kurr worth more in value but equal in measure? Actually, the text says 'a good quality kurr for two bad kurrs, and the pledge is worth one and a half of them'. So the good kurr is worth 1.5 bad kurrs. Then the pledge perishes.
Note that the measure (kurr) is the same unit, but quality differs.
2
State Zufar's position
Zufar: Quality is ignored in ribawi goods. So the good kurr is treated as equivalent in measure to a bad kurr. Therefore, it extinguishes one bad kurr (half the debt) because it is a pledge for two, half for each. The mortgagee still owes the other bad kurr.
This is a straightforward proportional extinguishment based on measure.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf: Quality has value outside commutative contracts, and a pledge is not a sale. So the mortgagee has an option: either guarantee the like (i.e., pay the value of the lost good kurr and take his debt) or take the better of the two debt kurrs (i.e., keep one bad kurr and give the other?) Actually, the text says 'take the better of the two kurrs and give the rest'. More clearly: he may keep the good kurr as satisfaction for one bad kurr plus some compensation, or return the equivalent.
The option gives flexibility to the mortgagee.
4
State Muhammad's position
Muhammad gives a detailed calculation: The pledged kurr is worth 300 dirhams, the debt two kurrs worth 200 dirhams. When the pledge loses 100 dirhams in quality (while measure unchanged), he prorates between secured and unsecured portions. The mortgagee owes a kurr worth 250 dirhams. This treats part of the quality excess as trust (amanah) and part as secured.
The calculation is more complex, considering that only part of the quality excess is secured by the debt.
5
Compare the practical outcomes
Zufar's view leads to simple proportional extinguishment. Abu Yusuf gives the mortgagee a choice. Muhammad's view requires a split calculation. The practical difference: under Zufar, the mortgagee gets 1 bad kurr; under Abu Yusuf, he may get 1 bad kurr plus compensation or take the better kurr; under Muhammad, he gets a specific calculated amount.
The text does not give a tarjih (preferred view), so later Hanafi practice may vary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح رهن الحجرين والمكيل والموزون والمراد بالحجرين الذهب والفضة، وإنما جاز رهن هذه الأشياء لإمكان الاستيفاء منها فكانت محلاً للرهن. وفي المبسوط : إذا كان الرهن مثل الدين كيلاً أو وزناً أو أكثر قيمة مثل قيمته أو كثر ذهب بما فيه لأنه صار مستوفياً لمثل حقه وإن كان أقل قيمة منه لم يذهب بالدين ويضمن المرتهن مثله ويأخذ منه دينه، وكذلك إذا فسد ولو رهنه كحنطة يساوي مائة بكر دقيق يساوي مائة فضاع الدقيق دفع المرتهن مثله ولم يذهب بالحنطة لأنه أقل كيلاً منها، وكذلك إذا فسد أو رهنه كراً جيداً بكرين رديئين والرهن يساوي كراً ونصفاً منها فهلك قال زفر رحمه الله تعالى : يذهب بكر رديء لأنه لا عبرة بالوجود في أموال الربا فصار الكر الجيد رهناً بكرين ردئين نصفه بهذا ونصه بذاك. وقال أبو يوسف : إن شاء ضمنه مثلي كره وأعطاه الدين، وإن شاء الكر بأجد الكرين وأعطاه الباقي لأن الجودة في أموال الربا لها قيمة في غير عقود المعاوضات والرهن عقد استيفاء لا معاوضة حقيقة فصار كمن له الجياد إذا استوفى الرديء ومن له الرديء إذا استوفى الجياد وهلك له أن يرد المقبوض ويستوفي حقه منه فهذا على ذلك. وقال محمد رحمه الله : رجل رهن رجلاً كراً من طعام قيمته ثلاثمائة درهم بكرين قيمتهما مائتان فأصاب الكر الرهن كان منه مائة مضمونة ما نقصه مائة وكيله واف على حاله فعلى المرتهن كر يساوي مائتي درهم وخمسين درهماً لأن الكر الرهن كان منه مائة مضمونة بأحد كري الدين وكانت إحدى هاتين المائتين مضمونة بأحد كري الدين والمائة الأخرى ليست بمضمونة فكان في الرهن فضل مائتين في الجودة وقيمتها ثلاثمائة فمائة منها مضمونة والمائة الأخرى أمانة، فلما أصابه بالنقص من جودته مائة جعلنا نصفها من الأمانة ونصفها
And the pledge of the two stones and measurable and weighable items is valid, meaning gold and silver. These items are permissible for pledge because it is possible to recover (the debt) from them, so they are fit for pledge. And in al-Mabsut: If the pledge is like the debt in measure or weight, or more in value like its value or more, it extinguishes (the debt) because he has received the like of his right. But if it is less in value, it does not extinguish the debt, and the mortgagee guarantees its like and takes his debt. Similarly if it spoils. And if he pledged something like wheat worth one hundred with a full measure of flour worth one hundred, and the flour is lost, the mortgagee pays its like and it does not extinguish the wheat because it is less in measure. Similarly if it spoils. Or if he pledged a good quality kurr for two bad quality kurrs, and the pledge is worth one and a half of them, and it perishes. Zufar (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It extinguishes one bad kurr, because quality is not considered in ribawi commodities, so the good kurr becomes a pledge for two bad kurrs, half for this and half for that. Abu Yusuf said: He may either guarantee its like and pay the debt, or (if he wishes) he may take the better of the two kurrs and give the rest (to the debtor), because quality in ribawi commodities has value outside commutative contracts, and a pledge is a contract of satisfaction, not a true commutative contract, so it is like one who has good quality and receives bad quality, or who has bad and receives good and it perishes; he may return what he received and take his right from it. So this is similar. Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: A man pledges a kurr of food worth three hundred dirhams for two kurrs worth two hundred. The pledged kurr suffers a loss of one hundred in quality while its measure remains the same. The mortgagee owes a kurr worth two hundred and fifty dirhams, because the pledged kurr had one hundred (measure) secured by one of the two debt kurrs, and one of those two hundred (value) was secured by the one debt kurr, and the other hundred was not secured. So in the pledge there was an excess of two hundred in quality worth three hundred; one hundred of that was secured and the other hundred was a trust. When it suffered a loss of one hundred in quality, we attribute half to the trust and half...
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage first confirms that gold, silver, measurable, and weighable items are valid subjects of pledge because their value can be realized. Then it quotes al-Mabsut on the general rule: if the pledged item matches the debt in measure/weight or exceeds in value, it extinguishes the debt upon loss; if less, the mortgagee guarantees the like. The core of this passage is the intra-Hanafi dispute among Zufar, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding a scenario where the pledged item is of higher quality but different quantity than the debt. Zufar argues quality is ignored in ribawi goods, so the loss extinguishes proportionally. Abu Yusuf offers an option: the mortgagee may either pay compensation or take a better quality item in settlement. Muhammad gives a detailed calculation considering secured and unsecured portions of the pledge. The text is cut off, but the general principle is that when the pledged item is of the same genus but different quality, the scholars differ on how to adjust the debt.

Key Terms
ربوي (ribawi): Commodities subject to riba rules (e.g., gold, silver, wheat, barley) where equality and hand-to-hand transfer are required.
جودة (jawdah): Quality; higher or lower grade of a ribawi commodity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Zufar, if a good quality kurr is pledged for two bad quality kurrs and perishes, how much of the debt is extinguished?
A) The entire one good kurr extinguishes one bad kurr.
B) The entire one good kurr extinguishes both bad kurrs.
C) Nothing is extinguished; the mortgagee must pay.
D) It extinguishes half of each bad kurr.

Key Takeaway
The three views show how quality is treated in pledge, a nuanced area of ribawi goods; no single tarjih is given here.
